# Clinton Francis Barton  —  *Hawkeye (also: Ronin, Goliath - briefly, Golden Archer - briefly)*

![[_attachments/clint_barton.png]]

# Clint Barton — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER: BARTON, CLINTON FRANCIS
## Executive Brief (BLUF)

---

### Bottom Line, Up Front

**Subject is a chronically depressed, complex-trauma-bearing high-functioning adult survivor of severe childhood abuse, operating as a peak-human craftsman in a peer environment of demigods, sustained by a hyperactive conscience and a self-concept fixed since age nine, whose entire adult career is legible as an extended, recursive act of penance for the unforgivable crime of having survived.** He is not a soldier, not a spy, not an assassin, and only nominally a superhero in the genre sense. He is a **journeyman moral laborer** who has chosen — and the word "chosen" is doing the heaviest lifting in this entire file — to spend a six-decade working life carrying the weight that more powerful figures are too large to notice and that less damaged figures are too whole to require. He is the most analytically transparent subject in the Avengers cohort and the most resistant to intervention, because the structure he is suffering inside is the same structure that makes him useful, and he knows it.

### The Through-Line

Trace the subject from Waverly, Iowa, in 1964 to the present and a single sentence describes every frame: **he keeps walking into rooms where he is told he does not belong, and he keeps proving it by staying.** The carnival accepts him and his brother as runaway labor; his mentors beat and abandon him; Black Widow flips him before he has been a hero for a full afternoon; Captain America drafts him into a team explicitly built of misfits and second-stringers; he founds a West Coast franchise of the Avengers that the East Coast house treats as a junior varsity; he marries a woman whose competence in his field exceeds his own and loses her to a moral standoff he refuses, even in retrospect, to surrender; he dies in a hallway with no audience; he comes back to a world that has redistributed his roles; he goes to Bed-Stuy and fights organized crime over a rent dispute because there is no smaller stage available and he requires a stage that is small. The arc does not curve. **It oscillates.** The longitudinal signature is not growth but **iteration on a fixed axis** — exile, return, restitution, exile — and the axis itself never moves.

The through-line, then, is not a trajectory. It is a **standing wave**. Subject has been the same man, in essential outline, since approximately the moment in early adolescence when he realized that the brother who was protecting him was also going to betray him. Every adult crisis is a re-litigation of that moment. Every relationship is a controlled experiment to see whether *this* attachment figure will, like all the others, turn. Every mission is a small payment toward a debt whose principal balance he has never been told and which, on the evidence, he believes to be infinite.

### The Core Wound

The core wound is not the father's violence. The father's violence is the *medium*. The core wound is the **structural lesson** the medium taught: **the people who are supposed to love you will hurt you, and the people who hurt you will be the only people available to love you, and there is no third category.** Harold Barton drank and hit; Edith Barton watched and did not intervene; Barney protected and then exploited; Swordsman taught and then beat; Trick Shot mentored and then hunted. By the time the subject was old enough to make an independent attachment choice, the schema was set in concrete: **intimacy is the warning sign for imminent harm, and the absence of harm is the warning sign for imminent abandonment.** Either the blow lands or the person leaves. There is no stable third state, because in the formative environment, no stable third state existed.

This schema does what schemas do: it generalizes. In adulthood it produces a man who **cannot let an attachment stabilize**. Stability triggers the precondition for the next blow; the nervous system, trained on a decade of unreliable caregivers, reads calm as the gathering before the storm and unconsciously precipitates the storm to control its timing. This is the engine behind every documented relational collapse in the file — Bobbi, Natasha, the brief and disastrous entanglements with Wanda and others, the deliberate construction of the Kate Bishop relationship along strictly asexual mentor-apprentice lines as a *protective measure*, the only one of his major attachments he has successfully prevented himself from destroying. Even the relationship with Captain America, the one stable male attachment of his life, was *initially* tested to destruction in the Kooky Quartet period — subject spent the better part of two years actively trying to provoke Cap into the abandonment or assault that would confirm the schema. Cap refused. **That refusal is the single most important external event in the subject's adult life**, and the subsequent loyalty to Cap is calibrated to its magnitude.

### Maintenance of the Wound

A wound this old, in a man this intelligent, would normally have been processed, medicated, dissolved, or at minimum named. The subject's has not been, and the file shows in detail *how* he keeps it intact:

1. **Refusal of the diagnostic vocabulary.** Subject has access to S.H.I.E.L.D., Avengers, and Stark-tier medical and psychiatric resources and has used none of them for the presenting problem in any sustained way. He will accept a sling for a broken arm. He will not accept a diagnosis for a broken childhood. The vocabulary of psychology is, in his self-presentation, *something other people use*, and his refusal of it is one of the most actively maintained features of his identity.

2. **Self-deprecating humor as the primary linguistic operating system.** The wisecrack does double duty: it pre-empts the assessment by performing it badly, and it makes the wound *legible only as joke*. Anyone trying to engage the underlying material has to first dismantle a fortification of comedy, and the comedy is good enough that the average interlocutor settles for laughing.

3. **Action-as-avoidance, ritualized.** The "I'll just go fight some guys" reflex is not a coping mechanism; it is a **liturgy**. It is performed at predictable intervals after predictable triggers, it has a predictable structure (exile, low-stakes vigilante work, injury, return), and it functions specifically to **substitute somatic exhaustion for affective processing**. The body absorbs what the psyche refuses.

4. **Cyclical penance.** Each major event generates a corresponding penitential exile. Bobbi's death, the Banner killing, every Barney reappearance, the various team disbandments — each precipitates a withdrawal into a smaller-scale, more thankless protectorate. The penances do not retire the debt; they **service the interest** on a principal that the subject refuses to examine.

5. **The chosen identity of "the regular guy."** Insisting on the bow-and-arrow framing, the carnie-kid framing, the no-powers framing, accomplishes two things simultaneously: it explains in advance why he will eventually fail (managing expectations downward to a survivable level), and it preserves the underdog identity that the childhood schema requires. **He needs to be the one nobody bet on, because the one nobody bet on is the only identity he was ever issued.**

6. **Selective hearing — literal and figurative.** The auditory disability is overdetermined. Self-inflicted in origin, deferred in treatment, intermittently restored and re-lost across canon, it functions as a **somatic externalization of his relationship to communication itself**: he has trouble hearing, he has trouble being heard, and the hardware of the problem is in his own skull. He treats the disability with the same deflection he treats every other interior matter, which is to say he integrates it into the joke and refuses to mourn it.

The wound, in sum, is **maintained because dismantling it would require the subject to construct a self he has no template for.** The depressive identity is painful but coherent; the alternative is unmapped. Like many trauma survivors, he prefers the legible suffering to the illegible recovery.

### Operational Summary

**Capability envelope:** Elite, narrow, sustainable. World-class small-arms marksman with primary specialization in archery at any range up to and exceeding documented human limits; secondary specialization in improvised projectile and trick-shot work using arbitrary objects; tertiary capability in close-quarters combat at a level adequate for sustained survival against augmented opponents but not at a level competitive with specialist combatants (Romanoff, Barnes, Rogers). Tactical and small-unit leadership rated **considerably higher than his self-presentation suggests** — West Coast Avengers tenure is the controlling evidence and is unambiguous. Improvisational problem-solving rated elite. Intelligence rated significantly above presented baseline; subject performs downward by approximately one standard deviation as a matter of identity, not capacity.

**Reliability profile:** **Extremely high on mission, extremely high on loyalty, moderate-to-low on self-care, low on disclosure.** Will execute. Will execute past the point any rational actor would. Will not report injury, will not report distress, will not request relief, will not surface degraded function until function has visibly collapsed. The pattern is consistent across six decades and is not amenable to instruction.

**Failure modes, in order of operational concern:** (1) **Martyrdom-seeking under guilt load** — subject will engineer high-cost self-sacrificial outcomes when survivor-guilt or moral-injury indices are elevated, regardless of mission necessity; (2) **Override of tactical sense in presence of children or civilians** — non-negotiable, weaponizable by adversaries who understand the trigger; (3) **Brother-induced destabilization** — any Barney Barton appearance produces decision-quality degradation lasting weeks; (4) **Cascade collapse following relational rupture** — the Stage-1-through-Stage-5 escalation is so reliably documented it can be predicted to within days of the precipitating event; (5) **Refusal of replacement or rotation** — will resist being pulled from an assignment he has self-defined as penitential, even when continued deployment is counterproductive.

**Handling notes:** Do not flatter; he distrusts it and it confirms the imposter framing. Do not pity; he will absorb it as more debt. **Do** assign clear, finite tasks with bounded scope and verifiable completion criteria; he is exceptional at these and they give him the brief experience of debt-retirement that the schema otherwise denies him. **Do** route him through Captain America (or, in Rogers's absence, the Rogers-coded figure he most respects in the moment) for any directive requiring acceptance of self-protective action; the only authority that overrides the self-punishment loop is the internalized voice of Steve Rogers, and the file shows it works every time it is used. **Do** preserve and protect the Kate Bishop relationship at institutional cost if necessary; it is the load-bearing wall of his current psychological architecture and its loss would not be recoverable. **Do not** task him alongside Barney Barton without contingency planning. **Do not** task him on missions with high collateral-civilian probability when guilt indices are already elevated; the martyrdom risk compounds.

**Replacement value:** **By power: low. By character: irreplaceable, and the institution does not appear to fully understand this.** Subject occupies a specific moral role inside the Avengers ecosystem — the **human-scale conscience**, the one whose presence forces the cosmic actors to remember the cost in apartment buildings and bystanders — that no other roster member fills. Romanoff is too compartmentalized for it; Rogers is too elevated; Stark is too implicated; Banner is too internal. **The team needs a witness at human scale, and Barton is the only one who has consented to be one.** He believes he is the most replaceable Avenger. He is, in the specific role he actually performs, the least.

### The One-Sentence File

**Clinton Francis Barton is a man who learned in childhood that he was not supposed to survive, has spent six adult decades trying to earn the survival anyway, has done so with such consistency and at such personal cost that the work has become indistinguishable from the wound, and will continue to do it — at the same iterative cadence, with the same refusals, the same humor, the same penance, the same exile-and-return — until the body fails or the people who depend on him release him from the obligation, neither of which event he will permit himself to anticipate.**

He is not in crisis. **He is the crisis, stabilized into a career.** And the stabilization is the achievement, and the achievement is also the diagnosis, and any plan to help him must begin from the recognition that he has organized his entire adult existence around the proposition that he does not deserve to be helped.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER: BARTON, CLINTON FRANCIS
## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

---

### 1.0 Birth, Geography, and the Negative Space of Records

Subject born **Clinton Francis Barton**, Waverly, Iowa — a small Bremer County town on the Cedar River, population then under 7,000, an agricultural-light-industrial seat with a Lutheran college, a grain elevator, and the demographic profile of the mid-century rural Midwest: white, Protestant, working-poor, insular. The choice of birth-site matters: Waverly is a place where everybody knows what is happening behind the curtains in the Barton house on the wrong side of the rail line and nobody, in canon or in extratextual inference, ever calls anyone. The Bartons are the family the neighbors do not invite over and do not interfere with — the rural-American compact of *we do not see what we see.* Establishing canon: *Solo Avengers* #1 (Mantlo/Lee, December 1987), reinforced with finer-grain detail in *Hawkeye: Blind Spot* #1–4 (Lapham, 2011) and in flashback throughout the Fraction/Aja *Hawkeye* (2012–2015), particularly issue **#19 (the ASL "barn" issue)** and the flashback structure of issues **#15** and **#22**.

No reliable birth certificate appears in canon. No baby photographs. Across thousands of pages of published material, **the Barton family album effectively does not exist** — a documentary absence which is itself the document. What we have of Clint's earliest years is what Clint remembers, and what Clint remembers is structured around the man he was trying to survive.

**Father: Harold Barton.** Occupation variously rendered as butcher (most consistent) and laborer; a man with a meat-counter apron, big-knuckled hands, and a routine. Drank. Drank specifically *after work*, the working-man's permission structure for nightly intoxication. The depicted alcoholism is not the bohemian sort; it is the corner-tavern-then-home sort, the predictable kind, the kind a child can set a clock to and therefore the kind that conditions a child's nervous system to a schedule of fear. **Mother: Edith Barton.** Functionally a non-intervener. The canon depicts her as present and affectionate in brief flashes (Lapham, *Blind Spot*), and otherwise as the person *not stopping it.* Whether from her own fear, her own learned helplessness, her own injuries, or her own complicity, the operational truth from inside the child's experience is identical: she did not pull him out of the way.

**Older brother: Bernard "Barney" Barton.** Approximately three years Clint's senior. In the household hierarchy, Barney occupied the role of pre-emptive shield and intermittent surrogate aggressor — the first instance of a structural pattern that will recur in every significant male relationship of Clint's life: *the same hand that protects also strikes.*

### 1.1 The Household — Ambient Conditions, Reconstructed

A full surveillance reconstruction of the pre-orphaning Barton household yields the following operating environment, derived from canonical flashbacks and the diagnostic residues visible in adult Clint's behavior:

- **Acoustic environment.** A house in which a raised voice was the dominant signal. Doors slammed. The specific cadence of a drunk man on stairs becomes — and remains, into adult Clint's life — a *startle prime*. (His later, somewhat darkly comic, hyper-attunement to footsteps on his own apartment stairs in Fraction's run is not stylistic; it is the same nervous-system tuning, repurposed.)
- **Olfactory environment.** Cheap whiskey or beer; cigarette smoke; the iron-and-fat smell of a butcher's clothes brought home unwashed. Adult Clint's notable aversion to certain bar environments and his strict moderation around hard liquor are downstream.
- **Spatial environment.** A small frame house. Bedroom shared with Barney — the foundational fact of the brothers' bond and its claustrophobia. Hiding places mapped in childhood, in the way that abused children always map them: under the porch, behind the barn, the loft, the culvert by the road, a particular spot in a stand of trees. Clint's lifelong preference for **high vantage** — rooftops, rafters, the literal *upper register* of any tactical situation — has its prototype in a child climbing up where the adult could not, or would not, follow.
- **Temporal environment.** A household keyed to Harold's shift, Harold's pay cycle, Harold's drinking schedule. Friday was worse than Tuesday. Payday was worse than the day before payday, and the day after payday was worst of all. **Clint Barton learned to read a calendar of danger before he learned to read.** This is the substrate of his adult tactical sense: time-of-day awareness, anticipation of when a situation will turn, the gut clock that tells him a thing is about to happen forty-five seconds before it does. His teammates marvel at it. It was installed in him before he was eight.

### 1.2 The First Mentor Who Was Also the First Abuser: The Father

Harold Barton is Clint's first male instructor in two subjects: **how to take a hit**, and **how a man is supposed to behave.** Both lessons are formative and both are inverted in Clint's adult life — he takes hits with the studied competence of a man who started his apprenticeship early, and he behaves, with women, with subordinates, with the vulnerable, **as a deliberate photographic negative of his father.** The negation is the architecture. Every drink Clint refuses, every partner he does not raise his voice to, every kid he speaks to gently, is Harold not happening again.

Canonical specificity: Harold is depicted striking both boys (*Blind Spot* #1; flashback fragments in Fraction *Hawkeye* #19). Edith is depicted as bruised and silent. The household is depicted as a place from which the brothers explicitly want to flee — the *Blind Spot* arc is structured around Clint and Barney as already-runaway-minded children long before the accident makes the question moot.

### 1.3 The Accident

Both parents killed in a drunk-driving crash. Harold at the wheel, drunk; Edith in the passenger seat; the boys, in the canonical telling, not in the car. The car goes off the road. Clint is, depending on the telling, somewhere between six and ten years old. Barney, three years older, takes operational charge of his brother within hours.

The diagnostic content of this event is not the bereavement. **The diagnostic content is the ambivalence of the bereavement.** The man who hit him is dead. The woman who did not stop him is dead. Relief is the forbidden emotion, and Clint, who is too young to have a vocabulary for what he feels, will spend the next half-century unable to name it and unable to put it down. The adult signature of this unprocessable ambivalence: his pathological inability to accept the death of anyone he has loved as a *closed file.* Bobbi, Cap, Banner — none of these losses are metabolized; all of them are carried, because the original loss was never permitted to be felt cleanly.

The institutional response is brief — county placement, an orphanage or group home in some tellings (*Solo Avengers* #1 implies an orphanage; later runs leave it vague). The brothers run, jointly and with intent, within weeks or months. **The flight is led by Barney.** Clint follows. The template of *Barney decides, Clint complies, Barney betrays, Clint absorbs* is set here, in the first independent decision of either of their lives.

### 1.4 The Brother

The Barney relationship is the most psychologically generative bond of Clint's childhood and the one whose structural defects produce the most adult damage.

In the household, Barney was the boy who stood between Harold and Clint when he could and who, when he could not — when he was himself being hit, when he was himself drunk on the inheritance of the same rage, when he was a teenager and Clint was a small child and Barney's tolerance had simply broken — turned on Clint himself. *Hawkeye* #19 (Fraction/Aja, the silent-and-ASL issue) renders this with extraordinary economy: the older Barney, wheelchair-bound, in the safehouse-barn with adult Clint, and the past keeps bleeding through into the present, and what they cannot say to each other in the present is exactly what they could not say to each other as children. **The brothers do not have language for each other.** They never did. The ASL is a literalization of a fact that was always true.

What Barney gave Clint, that the parents did not: continuity of presence, occasional protection, the only model of male affection he had until Captain America. What Barney took from Clint: trust in continuity itself. Barney leaves. Barney comes back. Barney leaves criminal. Barney fakes his death (*Avengers* #63–65, Thomas, 1969). Barney reappears. Barney tries to kill him. Barney is dying. Barney is not dying. The pattern is so consistent across publication history (1969, the late '80s solo work, *Blind Spot*, Fraction's run, *Hawkeye Freefall* 2020) that it functions as the **central recurring stimulus** of Clint's adult life: the one variable he cannot game-theory, because the variable was installed before he had a theory.

### 1.5 The Carnival — Carson Carnival of Travelling Wonders

The boys, on the run, attach to a traveling carnival. This is the single most determinative environment of Clint's adolescence and arguably of his life; everything visible in adult Clint that is not a Harold-negation is a carnival-positive.

**What the carnival is, as a developmental environment:**

- **A surrogate family of non-conventional adults.** Roustabouts, performers, freaks, grifters. The carnies do not ask where the boys came from; they do not file paperwork; they do not call the county. Clint learns here that *chosen family* is a category that exists, and that it is composed largely of people the respectable world has discarded. His lifelong affinity for the marginal, the working-class, the visibly damaged — his Bed-Stuy neighbors, the apartment-building tenants in Fraction, the Banner-killing aftermath where he refuses the celebrity defense team — descends directly from this stratum.
- **An economy of skill.** In the carnival, what you can *do* is what you are worth. The boy with the bow becomes the boy with the bow. This is the foundational reward schedule of Clint's life: **craft pays.** It pays in food, in standing, in the right to stay. The adult Clint who self-identifies as "a guy with a bow" is reciting a survival creed from age twelve.
- **An aesthetics of performance.** The wisecrack, the showmanship, the bright costume, the trick shot performed for an audience — these are not affectations imposed on Clint by the superhero genre; they are the **native dialect of the environment that raised him.** When he banters in combat, he is doing what his first job trained him to do: keep the audience's attention, sell the trick, never break the patter. The clown nose is welded to the bowstring at the level of professional formation.
- **A culture of itinerancy.** No fixed address. No permanent neighbors. The fairground is in town for a week and then it is not. Adult Clint's relationship to *place* — his repeated abandonments of stable arrangements, his "go dark" exile pattern, his treatment of an apartment lease as essentially a long carnival stop — is the carnival schedule, internalized.

### 1.6 The Second Father Who Was Also a Betrayer: The Swordsman

**Jacques Duquesne** — the Swordsman — takes Clint under instruction. The pedagogy is real: edged weapons, hand-to-hand, the physics of throwing, the discipline of practice. The bond is real. Clint, who has had a violent father and an unreliable brother, accepts a stylish French swordsman with a black mustache as a paternal substitute with the speed and totality of a child who has been starving.

Then Clint, who has good eyes and the kind of pattern-recognition that small children of alcoholics develop as a survival adaptation, **catches Duquesne embezzling from the carnival.** Duquesne beats him — in some renderings nearly to death, in others severely enough to require extended convalescence — and disappears. (Cf. *Avengers* #19 and later expansions; *Solo Avengers* #1.)

Diagnostic content: the boy learns, with experimental confirmation, that **noticing the truth about a beloved adult produces violence.** This is the moment that installs his lifelong reluctance to confront — directly, verbally, in the open — the failings of people he loves. He will go around. He will deflect with humor. He will leave town. He will not, except under extraordinary pressure, *say the thing.* The conversation he could not have with Bobbi about the Phantom Rider, the conversations he never has with Cap about his own resentments, the conversations he refuses to have with the various therapists every Avengers HR apparatus has tried to put him in front of — all descend from the lesson that **truth-telling to a loved adult is met with a beating.**

### 1.7 The Third Father Who Was Also a Betrayer: Trick Shot

**Buck Chisholm** — Trick Shot — picks up where Duquesne left off and gives Clint what is, in skill terms, the decisive instruction of his life: the bow. The technical foundation of every shot Clint Barton has ever made on panel was laid by Chisholm — stance, draw, release, the read of wind and distance, the discipline of the morning practice. The relationship is the most pedagogically substantive of his childhood.

It also ends in betrayal. Chisholm uses Clint in a criminal scheme; Clint refuses; Chisholm walks out, and later, as an adult antagonist, **actively tries to kill him** (*Hawkeye* limited series, Mantlo/Guice, 1983; reinforced in subsequent retellings). The pattern is now closed and triple-confirmed: **father, sword-mentor, bow-mentor — three adult men, three abusers/abandoners.** By the time Clint is approximately fifteen to seventeen years old, his complete empirical sample of trusted adult men has betrayed him at a base rate of 100%.

A child cannot reason from this without concluding something is wrong with the child. Clint concludes it. He will spend his adult life trying to disconfirm a hypothesis that was installed by a sample size too small to evaluate but too consistent to disbelieve.

### 1.8 Earliest Behavioral Patterns — A Catalogue

By the end of the childhood stratum — late adolescence, immediately pre-costume — the following patterns are observable in latent or active form. Each will be diagnostic for the remainder of his life.

1. **Hyper-vigilant time-and-space awareness**, manifesting in childhood as the calendar-of-danger and the map-of-hiding-places; in adulthood as elite tactical anticipation, preference for high ground, and a habit of scanning rooflines on entry to any new space.
2. **High-vantage preference** — climbed where adults would not follow; will, as an adult, occupy literal and figurative high ground in nearly every depicted action sequence.
3. **The wisecrack as affect management**, learned in the carnival as professional patter and ratified by the household as a way to defuse a man whose hand was about to come up.
4. **Craft-as-worth.** Whatever you can do is what you are. The bow is the membership card.
5. **Counter-dependence.** Asking for help got Edith hurt and got Clint hit. Doing it yourself is the only safe operating mode. The adult signature: "I'm just the guy with no powers," delivered as a boast and meaning *please do not require me to need any of you.*
6. **Loyalty to chosen family over institutions.** The carnival was not the State. The carnies were not the orphanage. The Avengers will be the carnival. SHIELD will be the orphanage.
7. **An affinity for the marginal.** The people the respectable world discards are his people, because they were the ones who took him in.
8. **Inability to confront loved adults directly.** Installed by Duquesne's response to being caught. Will define every romantic relationship he attempts.
9. **A self-concept calibrated to a 100% betrayal sample.** *Something is wrong with me; that is why they all left.* This is the dysthymic floor under the wisecracks, audible from his first appearance.
10. **A protective relationship to anyone framed as a younger version of himself.** The child Clint did not get protected. The adult Clint will overprotect, overextend, and overcommit to any sufficiently similar surrogate. Kate Bishop is the well-managed case. The cases that are not Kate are where he gets hurt worst.
11. **Refusal of cruelty as tactical option** — the Harold negation, absolute. He will lose fights he could win by being cruel, and he will lose them on purpose.
12. **Vigilant moderation around alcohol.** He has watched the genetic film already. He knows the ending.
13. **A startle-response to the cadence of a drunk man's footfall** that he has never, in canon, named but which is observable in panel any time the stimulus appears.
14. **The performance smile.** The carnival face. Worn into combat, into press conferences, into hospital rooms. Off only when he is alone, asleep, or with Kate.

### 1.9 Closing Note on the Stratum

What is constructed by approximately age eighteen — pre-costume, pre-Iron-Man-inspiration, pre-Natasha, pre-Avengers — is the **complete chassis** of the adult Clint Barton. The superhero career does not build him; it furnishes him. Every later betrayal (Mockingbird's secret, Barney's recurrences, the various mentor-figures who go bad) lands on a foundation that was poured before he could read. Every later devotion (Cap, Kate, the apartment building, the bow itself) is the carnival's gift, re-spent.

The childhood stratum is therefore not background to the dossier. **It is the dossier.** Everything subsequent is annotation.


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

## The Carnival Years (Approximate Ages 9–17)

### Arrival and Intake

Subject and elder brother Bernard ("Barney") Barton enter the Carson Carnival of Travelling Wonders following placement at, and flight from, a state-run group home in or near Waverly, Iowa. Canon is consistent that the boys ran rather than aged out; behavior file of the period (inferred from corroborating dialogue, *Solo Avengers* #1, *Hawkeye: Blind Spot* #1–4, *Hawkeye: Freefall* #4) is consistent with two minors who had already learned that adult institutions were to be evaded rather than trusted. Subject is the smaller, younger, and more biddable of the pair. Barney is the negotiator at the carnival gate. This relational division — Barney as the front-of-house, Clint as the labor — persists for the entirety of the carnival stratum and is the template for every subsequent peer-equal relationship subject will fail at.

The carnival's economy in this era (mid-rural-circuit travelling tent show, declining post-television model) ran on cheap juvenile labor in exchange for a cot, a meal, and notional protection from authorities. Subject's labor intake: rigging, animal mucking, tent canvas, ticket runner, eventually warm-up and prop work for the marquee acts. Standard six-day work week, irregular hours, no schooling beyond what Barney taught him to read from comic books and posters. The literacy gap is permanent and shows up later in spelling tics across his handwritten Avengers reports — phonetic, capable, never academic.

### The First Mentor — The Swordsman

Jacques Duquesne, billed as the Swordsman, becomes subject's first chosen father-figure within approximately eighteen months of arrival. Selection criteria are diagnostic: Duquesne is theatrical, French-accented, gracious in performance, casually cruel offstage, and a drinker. **The chosen father reproduces the biological father almost feature-for-feature**, minus the marriage and plus the blade work. Subject does not perceive this; few children do.

The training regimen, reconstructed from depicted and recalled material:

- **Fundamentals of edged weapon work** — footwork, distance management, the discipline of the held line. Subject demonstrates immediate aptitude for the *spatial* components (range estimation, angle, timing) and indifferent aptitude for the *bladed* components proper.
- **Acrobatics and high-wire work**, partly because the Swordsman's act needed a foil and partly because the carnival rented training time across disciplines.
- **Stage discipline** — hitting marks, working a crowd, the choreography of pretend danger that conceals real danger. Subject's later combat patter, his almost compulsive narration during fights, originates here. He learned that an audience needs to be talked to or it gets nervous.

The training is the first sustained adult attention subject has ever received that is not violent on arrival. He attaches with the totality only an under-attached child can muster. There is no indication in canon that Duquesne was sexually inappropriate; the betrayal, when it comes, is financial and physical, which is bad enough.

### The Second Mentor — Trick Shot

Buck Chisholm, billed as Trick Shot, is recruited or contracted into subject's training at the Swordsman's suggestion — ostensibly to develop a secondary act for the boy, in practice because Duquesne had identified a marketable child prodigy and wanted to diversify the investment. **The bow is therefore, at point of origin, not subject's first love; it is a job assignment.** This is significant for the entire later identity. He did not pick up the bow because he loved the bow. He picked it up because two adults told him to and the bow turned out to love him back.

Trick Shot's curriculum, reconstructed:

- **Recurve and traditional longbow** before any compound work; finger release before mechanical.
- **The full carnival trick repertoire**: William Tell variants, blindfold work, mirror shots, candle snuffs, splitting an arrow already in the target, the moving-platform shot, the back-shot from a thrown bow. Each of these later appears, weaponized, in his Avengers tenure. The mirror shot in particular (line-of-sight via reflective surface) becomes a signature move whose origin is a fifteen-foot carnival mirror in a 1970s Indiana fairground.
- **Wind reading, light reading, ground reading** — the unromantic, deeply technical substrate that the "natural marksman" framing later obscures.
- **The internal stillness**. Trick Shot's actual gift to subject, the one that outlasted the betrayal: the breathing pattern, the body-quiet, the capacity to drop heart rate before release. This is the only piece of childhood instruction subject still uses every day of his adult life, and it is the piece least visible to anyone watching him.

Subject's draw weight progression — inferred from depicted bow poundages in adult life (70–125 lb military draws on specialty bows) — required years of progressive musculoskeletal loading begun pre-puberty. His shoulders and back were built by this work. So was the rotator cuff damage discussed in Section 2.

By approximately age fifteen, subject is the marquee juvenile act: **the World's Greatest Marksman**, billed under that title or close variants. The grandiosity of the billing matters. It is the first time anyone has told him he is the *best* at anything. He believes it operationally (he can do the shots) and disbelieves it constitutionally (he is still the carnie kid, still Harold Barton's son). This split — *demonstrated excellence the subject cannot internalize* — becomes the permanent architecture of his self-concept.

### The Embezzlement and the Beating

The triggering rupture (*Avengers* #19 backstory, expanded *Hawkeye: Blind Spot*): subject discovers Duquesne skimming the night's gate. The discovery is accidental — he was looking for Duquesne for an ordinary reason, found him in the wrong tent, saw the wrong thing. Duquesne, drunk, offers him a cut to stay quiet. Subject refuses. Subject is then beaten with sufficient force to require extended convalescence; canon variously describes broken ribs, concussion, facial fractures.

Two facts about this beating are forensically critical:

1. **Subject's first major lesson about institutional loyalty was that telling the truth to the wrong adult gets you broken.** He has, in the four decades of published material since, *never once* gone to a superior with information against a peer without first attempting to handle it himself. The pattern that gets labeled "loner" or "insubordinate" originates here.
2. **He refused the bribe.** Under physical threat from his only father-figure, having no alternative living arrangement, he refused. The hyperactive conscience that Section 3 identifies is present at age fourteen-or-so, fully operational, and was forged in that tent. Whatever else broke that night, the moral spine did not.

Duquesne disappears from the carnival the same night. Subject recovers without proper medical care, which is to say improperly. The first of the deferred medical debts.

### The Trick Shot Betrayal

Chisholm's defection is staggered and worse. Where Duquesne fled, Trick Shot **stays**, and the relationship becomes one of escalating disillusionment as subject begins to understand that his second mentor is a contract killer who used the carnival as cover. The exact sequencing varies by retelling, but the through-line is consistent: Trick Shot attempts to recruit subject into paid criminal work; subject refuses; Trick Shot eventually takes a contract that places him in opposition to subject in adult life (this pays off across *Solo Avengers* and the Fraction-adjacent material).

The damage is structurally different from the Swordsman's. Duquesne hurt him with a sudden act. Chisholm hurt him by being, slowly and over months, *not the person subject had decided he was*. **Subject's adult difficulty with re-assessment of trusted figures** — the way he holds onto people past the point of evidence (Barney, Bobbi's secrets, Wanda's instability) — traces to this period. Having been wrong about Chisholm slowly and painfully, he developed an aversion to updating priors on intimates. It is cheaper to be hurt than to be wrong again.

## Barney's Departure

Bernard Barton leaves the carnival in or around subject's mid-adolescence. Canon vacillates on whether Barney left first (forcing Clint to find a new male attachment in Duquesne) or whether Barney left after the beating (in a version where Clint's wounds are blamed implicitly on his having stayed when Barney wanted them both gone). Both versions are operative in subject's internal narrative; he has, in different stories, blamed himself for both timelines.

Barney enters organized crime — initially low-level enforcement work, later FBI infiltration of same, the loyalties shifting issue to issue. Subject does not see him for years. The brother who taught him to read, fight, and steal vanishes, and is replaced in subject's life by the bow.

**This is the foundational substitution of subject's identity: the bow stands in for the brother.** The intensity of his craft practice — by all accounts obsessive even by professional standards, with depicted daily-arrow-counts in the four-figure range during training periods — is grief work. He has never identified it as such.

## Departure From the Carnival (Approximate Age 17–18)

Subject leaves the carnival circuit alone, on foot or by hitching, with a bow, a quiver, the clothes he wore, and no documentation. He has no high-school equivalency, no driver's license he didn't forge, no address, no living relative he can locate, and no marketable skill except a stage act for which the venue no longer exists. The carnival circuit was dying; the act was specialized; there was no civilian career path for **the World's Greatest Marksman** in 1970s America.

He works odd jobs across an undocumented period of months-to-years: itinerant labor, security at small venues, exhibition shots at sporting goods stores and gun shows (where his being a *bowman* rather than a shooter is a novelty draw). He sleeps rough or in shared rentals. He develops the cooking-from-a-hotplate, eating-out-of-the-can dietary patterns that persist into his Avengers years and that no amount of money ever fully reforms — Stark could buy him a private chef and he would still want the pizza.

This period is where the **populist sympathies** identified in Section 5 are cemented. He has now been, in sequence, an abused child, a runaway, a child laborer, a betrayed apprentice, and an undocumented itinerant adult. He has *experienced* the population he will later be reflexively protective of. The Bed-Stuy apartment building three decades later is not charity; it is recognition.

## Coney Island and the Wrong-Turn Origin

### The Inspiration

Subject witnesses Iron Man in action — variously rendered as a televised intervention, a public appearance, or a chance sighting. The detail is less important than the response: he watches a man who has chosen to be useful through equipment and decides, immediately and without consultation, *I can do that*. He has no powers. He has equipment-of-a-sort (the bow) and an act. He decides this is enough.

The choice is, in retrospect, slightly insane. The cost-benefit ratio for a non-powered human entering early-Marvel-era costumed crimefighting was catastrophic. Subject does not run the math. The decision is made on the affective register: *here is a way to be the kind of man who is on television for the right reasons instead of the wrong ones*. His father was on the local news once, after the accident. He would prefer a different headline.

### The Costume

The first Hawkeye costume — purple and blue, mask, the gauntlet quiver — is self-assembled. Subject sews. (He had to; carnival hands repair their own kit.) The color palette is consistent across all subsequent versions and is not arbitrary: purple was the cheapest stage-grade fabric dye that read as distinct from the existing Avengers/heroes color codes of the period. The pragmatic origin of the iconic palette is *what was on sale*.

The mask covers eyes only. Subject has, across his entire career, refused full-face concealment. The reasons are operational (peripheral vision, comms, the bow needs an unobstructed cheek anchor) and psychological (he is not hiding; he wants the wrong people to recognize him; on some level he wants Harold Barton's ghost to see who he became). He has also never used a voice modulator. The voice is the voice. He is, oddly for a costumed operator, almost incapable of true disguise — the Ronin identity later excepted, and Ronin is the exception that proves the rule (he took it because he was not, at that moment, willing to be Clint).

### The Coney Island Misidentification (*Tales of Suspense* #57, 1964)

The first costumed appearance: subject intervenes in a robbery or apparent crime at a Coney Island event. He is mistaken for the thief by responding police and by the crowd. The misidentification is not random — he is masked, armed, athletic, and arrived from the wrong direction. To a witness at fifty feet, *the man with the bow looks like the man who did the thing*.

Subject's response, forensically: **he runs.** He does not stay and explain. He does not surrender to clarify. He flees. This is the carnival reflex (authority is to be evaded) and the abused-child reflex (explaining will not work) operating in tandem at the moment of his public debut. **The first thing the world sees Hawkeye do is run from the police.** Every subsequent reading of him as outlaw-adjacent traces partly to this moment, which was an honest panic.

### The Black Widow Encounter

In flight, subject encounters Natalia Alianovna Romanova, then a fully active KGB asset operating in the United States against Stark Industries. She is approximately his age, beautiful, professionally competent, and *interested in him*. She has not, in his entire life, been preceded by any woman who has been all four of those things at once.

The recruitment is textbook, executed by an operator at the top of her tradecraft against a target with no countermeasures. She offers:

- **Belief in his innocence** — the first adult to do so in the public matter at hand.
- **A frame for his grievance** — Stark is the rich man, you are the poor one mistaken for a thief, the system is rigged, here is how we equalize it.
- **Physical proximity and the implication of more** — never quite consummated in this phase, kept as forward incentive.
- **A mission** — the thing he has wanted for years, which is to be told what to do by someone he can respect.

He folds inside an afternoon. The brevity is the diagnosis. He goes from costumed-debut to attacking Iron Man on behalf of a foreign intelligence asset in a sequence of decisions that takes hours, not days. **Pattern established at first appearance, present for the next sixty years of publication: poor situational judgment under interpersonal pressure; willingness to invert moral framework for a woman who shows him attention; reflexive identification with the underdog position even when wrong.**

### The Stark Engagements

The arc across *Tales of Suspense* #57, #60, #64 — subject and Romanova running a campaign of harassment against Stark/Iron Man — has been treated as origin pulp and is also a precise behavioral data set. Subject in this period:

- Demonstrates the full adult marksmanship skill set already; the carnival training transferred without degradation.
- Improvises trick arrows from off-the-shelf components — putty, magnet, suction-cup, acid, explosive — in a workshop he assembles himself. **The trick-arrow tradition begins here, as a one-man R&D operation,** and remains a one-man R&D operation for most of his career even after Stark resources are available. He prefers his own arrows. He prefers them because he made them and because they will work in the way he expects them to work, which is a control-issue inheritance from the carnival betrayals.
- Loses every direct engagement with Iron Man on points. Stark out-tech is decisive. Subject's response is not to disengage but to escalate, which is the Stage-3-of-five stress pattern documented in Section 5 already operational in his first published month.
- Is repeatedly rescued, redirected, or covered for by Romanova. He notices this and does not let it modify his behavior. He is, for these issues, in love and stupid with it.

The fallout, once Romanova is extracted and the campaign collapses: subject is left in the United States, criminally exposed, romantically abandoned, and operationally adrift. He has chosen, in his first major adult decision, the wrong side, for the wrong reason, against the wrong man, and lost. **The shame of this period is permanent.** It is the reason, decades later, he cannot quite take Tony Stark's authority seriously — not because he disrespects Tony, but because *he cannot face Tony as an equal without the memory of having shot at him for a girl*. Their later teammate dynamic is shaped end-to-end by an unaddressed apology subject has never figured out how to deliver.

## The Pivot — Approach to the Avengers

In the interval between the Stark campaign's collapse and his Avengers application (*Avengers* #16, 1965), subject makes the second defining choice of his young adulthood: rather than disappear, double down on the criminal frame, or seek out a new handler, he **walks up to Avengers Mansion and asks to join**.

The audacity of this is sometimes underread. He has, at this point:

- A public criminal record (the Coney Island misidentification, the Stark engagements).
- No powers.
- No team experience above the carnival-act level.
- A documented prior association with a foreign intelligence asset.
- Approximately one suit of homemade purple fabric and a bow.

He goes anyway. He talks his way past Jarvis (canon-implied; the specifics are off-panel and have been variously embroidered). He demonstrates, for the founding-tier Avengers, the shot — some version of the apple/coin/cable trick that was the carnival closer. **He auditions for the Avengers using a routine he learned from a man who tried to kill him.** The continuity of the bow across mentors, betrayals, romantic disasters, and reinvention is the through-line of the entire identity. The bow does not betray him.

Cap signs off. The reasons are Cap's; the *fact* of being signed off on by Captain America is, for subject, world-altering. **The first adult male authority figure to look at Clint Barton's record and choose him anyway**. The Kooky Quartet era begins.

## The Defining Choices and Their Fallout — A Summary Index

For dossier indexing, the formation-era choices that bind every subsequent decade:

1. **Going to the carnival rather than back to the state.** Established institutions as enemy, ad-hoc found-family as solution. Persists.
2. **Refusing the Swordsman's bribe under threat.** Established the conscience as more load-bearing than the body. Persists.
3. **Picking up the bow as job, keeping it as identity.** Established craft as substitute for connection. Persists.
4. **Running rather than explaining at Coney Island.** Established the flight reflex under authority's gaze. Persists, in modulated form, as the post-event exile pattern.
5. **Believing Romanova inside an afternoon.** Established the romantic-recruitment vulnerability. Recurs with every significant partner.
6. **Walking up to Avengers Mansion.** Established that subject, when at his lowest, reaches *up* rather than *out*. This is the choice that saves him, and it is the one piece of his formation that he has — to his own confusion — never been able to repeat. Every later attempt to reach up (to Cap, to the institution) is a reenactment of this one decision, and none has worked as cleanly. He has spent a career trying to walk back up to Avengers Mansion. He does not know that he is doing it.

The formation closes here. What emerges from the carnival and the Coney Island fiasco and the mansion doorway is not yet the Hawkeye of the leadership stratum or the Fraction stratum; it is the raw substrate. Every later iteration is a recombination of these elements. He never adds new ones. He could not. They were laid down too deep, too young, by too few people, almost all of whom hurt him.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*(Subject's approximate operational years 8 through 28: from the founding of the West Coast Avengers through the Ronin period and the immediate post-Resurrection re-integration. This is the stratum in which the patterns established in childhood and the early Avengers years harden into the operating system the Subject will run for the rest of his life. He will not become a different person after this period. He will only become more legible as the person he became during it.)*

---

## 3.1 The Westward Decision: Refusal of the Center

The pivot opens with a refusal. Offered the chairmanship of a new Avengers expansion team based in Los Angeles (*West Coast Avengers* limited series, 1984; ongoing #1, 1985), Subject does not decline — but the *acceptance* itself is the diagnostic event. By this point he has spent roughly a decade as a second-tier Avenger under Cap, Iron Man, and the Vision; the New York team is the institutional center, the prestige assignment, the place where the legend is written. He chooses the satellite. He chooses the desert. He chooses to be **the principal of his own school** rather than a teacher at someone else's.

Surveillance reading: this is the first time in his life Subject has been handed an institution and told to *run* it rather than test it. He does not sabotage the offer (the standard early-Avengers move) and he does not defer to a more obvious candidate (Wonder Man was available; Vision was available). He takes the chair. The decision is uncharacteristic on the surface and entirely consistent underneath: Los Angeles is **exile dressed as promotion**. He gets to lead without competing with Cap; he gets to be central without being watched by the man whose disapproval would unmake him. The geography is the defense.

Routine artifacts of this period as a surveillance file would capture them:

- **The compound.** A converted Palos Verdes estate funded by Stark. Subject's quarters are notably under-decorated relative to teammate suites — a bed, a workbench, a target range, a small framed photo of the Carson Carnival troupe, no plants, no books that are not technical (fletching manuals, bowyer's references, a battered copy of *The Art of War* dog-eared at the Sun Tzu sections he uses as wisecrack source material). The room is staged like a hotel room he expects to leave.
- **The range.** Built to his specifications behind the main house. Distances marked every ten yards out to 300. He shoots a minimum of 300 arrows per day, every day, before any other activity. Weather is irrelevant. The Santa Ana winds become a training asset he refers to in dialogue as "the cheating coach."
- **The recruiting calls.** Documented: Tigra (active recruitment, *WCA* #1), Wonder Man (already attached), Iron Man/Rhodes-era (loaned), Vision and Scarlet Witch (relocated). Undocumented but consistent with the institutional pattern: Subject was the one making the phone calls. Hank Pym joins as non-combat science support — Subject's choice, against the institutional grain, and the most important leadership decision of the entire West Coast era. Pym has just been court-martialed out of the East Coast team. Subject *recruits the disgraced man*. This is not charity; it is identification. He builds the West Coast team partly as a halfway house for Avengers the East Coast has finished with. Mockingbird (ex-SHIELD, burned), Pym (court-martialed), Tigra (between identities), Wonder Man (between deaths). The roster is a roster of damaged people. Subject is most comfortable as primus inter pares of a room in which everyone else also has a file.

## 3.2 The Marriage: Bobbi Morse

The marriage to **Bobbi Morse (Mockingbird)** is, by the file's accounting, the central interpersonal event of Subject's adult life — including everything that comes after. They marry in Las Vegas after a 48-hour acquaintance (*Hawkeye* vol. 1 #4, 1983; ratified as legitimate in the *WCA* limited, 1984). Almost every behavioral pattern Subject will display for the next thirty years either originates in this marriage or is sharpened by its collapse.

Granular observations the file would log:

- **Operational synchronization.** They train together at the compound range. He uses bow, she uses battle-staves; the range footage (if one imagines the surveillance) would show two people at completely different skill modalities developing an unspoken rotational drill — she moves into his shot lanes without checking, he releases knowing where she will be. By approximately 18 months in, they no longer call cover. This is the high-water mark of Subject's adult capacity for trust. He never operates with another partner at this density again. The closest comparison in later canon is the working rhythm with Kate Bishop, and Kate Bishop is specifically *not* a romantic partner — the file should note that the only lateral relationship Subject ever fully relaxed inside was one he has spent two decades refusing to recreate.
- **Domestic micro-patterns.** He cooks; she does not. He cooks badly — eggs, grilled cheese, anything that can be done over high heat in under six minutes. She brings home takeout three nights a week. They share a coffee maker that he services obsessively. They argue about thermostat settings. He sleeps on the left side of the bed; she on the right; he wakes earlier and goes to the range; she sleeps until 0830. They have a dog briefly (a shepherd mix, never named in canon, depicted in one *WCA* domestic interlude); the dog dies; they do not get another. He keeps the collar in a workbench drawer for years afterward.
- **The argument register.** Bobbi argues to win; Subject argues to *not lose*. She is verbally faster, has SHIELD-trained interrogation skills, and reads him at speed. He responds with humor that escalates to provocation that escalates to leaving the room. The diagnostic pattern: he does not slam doors. He closes them softly and goes to the range. The shooting after a fight is depicted (Englehart) as flawless, mechanical, and silent. He shoots his anger out at 70 yards.

### 3.2.1 The Phantom Rider Incident

In the *Lost in Space-Time* arc (*WCA* #17–24, 1987), the team is thrown into the Old West. Bobbi is captured, drugged, and sexually assaulted by **Lincoln Slade, the Phantom Rider**, who attempts to brainwash her into being his bride. She breaks free, allows him to fall to his death, and chooses *not to tell Subject* — a decision motivated, in narrative, by her recognition of exactly how he will react.

She is correct. When Subject learns the truth (*WCA* #100, 1993, in flashback structure), his response is the catastrophic event of his adult life. He does not condemn her for the killing in isolation — he condemns the *concealment*. He frames it as a violation of partnership ethics: she made a moral decision in the marriage's name without him, and she let him operate beside her under a false picture. The framing is, on the surface, principled. Beneath the surface it is something more diagnostic: **she handled a crisis without him, and that is the unforgivable thing.** A man whose entire identity is built on being needed by the people he loves cannot tolerate being kept *out* of one of their crises. The principle is real; it is also a cover for the wound.

The marriage ends not in divorce but in slow dissolution. They separate, work together intermittently, attempt reconciliation, fail. Subject moves through this period (1988–1993) in a depressive register that the West Coast scripts code as "moody." Behavioral artifacts:

- He stops cooking entirely.
- Coffee consumption doubles.
- He takes the *most* dangerous point positions on every mission, in a manner the file would flag as **passive suicidality with plausible deniability**.
- He grows the beard for the first time on record. (The beard will recur at every later depressive interval. It is a tell.)
- He drinks more, monitored against the Harold Barton baseline he carries in his head. Never crosses into impairment on mission. The discipline holds. The discipline is itself a symptom.

### 3.2.2 The Death

Bobbi dies in his arms in the burning Mephisto compound (*AWC* #100, 1993), having taken a hellfire blast intended for him. The death is a textbook moral-injury cocktail: she dies *for* him; she dies *before* the reconciliation he had begun to allow himself to imagine; she dies during a mission *he* led. He carries her body out. He does not speak for the remainder of the issue. He does not, in subsequent appearances, speak about it for years.

(File note: when Bobbi is later revealed to have been Skrull-replaced at some prior point and the "real" Bobbi recovered during *Secret Invasion*, 2008, Subject's response is not joy. It is destabilization. The grief he had calcified into identity now has nowhere to live. He cannot rebuild the marriage; he cannot mourn the wife. The second Bobbi return is, paradoxically, harder than the first death.)

## 3.3 The Dissolution and the Wandering Years (1989–1996)

The West Coast Avengers as an institution does not survive Subject's marriage. The team is dissolved by an East Coast Avengers vote (*Avengers* #354 / *Force Works* setup, 1994); Subject is bypassed. The bypass is read by him — accurately — as the institution telling him he was a custodian, not a chairman. **Iron Man drives the dissolution.** The hostility between Subject and Stark, which dates to the *Tales of Suspense* origin but which had been functionally dormant during the WCA years, reactivates and becomes the slow-burn antagonism that will define their relationship through the Registration era a decade later.

He does not join Force Works (the replacement team Stark co-founds). He drifts. The file logs:

- A brief return to the **Carson Carnival** circuit (*Hawkeye* vol. 2 limited, 1994), explicitly nostalgic, explicitly regressive. He works the trick-shot booth under his own name. He sleeps in a trailer. He drinks marginally more. He is photographed (in-universe) by a local Iowa paper and the image is the cleanest visual evidence of the depressive baseline: thirty-odd pounds of muscle on a frame that should carry forty, eyes that do not quite track the camera, the half-smile.
- A short stint running security for **Crossfire's** old territory in the Pacific Northwest, framed as undercover work, functionally a man taking the first job that didn't require him to be Hawkeye in front of anyone.
- Cameo Avengers appearances (Galactic Storm, the Gatherers crisis) as reservist. He is on the call sheet, not the org chart.

This wandering period is the **template for every later exile cycle**. The pattern that the file will see recur after Cap's death, after the Banner killing, after Freefall: *something breaks; he leaves; he takes a small, dirty, local job; he drinks more but not enough; he grows the beard; he returns when called and pretends the interval did not happen*. The West Coast dissolution is where the loop first runs end-to-end as an adult.

## 3.4 The Thunderbolts Chairmanship (1999–2000)

Subject takes leadership of the **Thunderbolts** (*Thunderbolts* #21, 1998, full chairmanship soon after) — a team of reformed (or reforming, or pretending to reform) supervillains. Cap is, at this point, alive and active; the East Coast team has reconstituted post-*Heroes Return*. Subject could rejoin. He chooses, again, the satellite — and this time the satellite is composed entirely of damaged people who explicitly need a halfway house. The recapitulation of the WCA recruiting logic is exact. Where WCA was a halfway house for ex-Avengers, the Thunderbolts are a halfway house for ex-villains.

The chairmanship is, by competence metrics, his finest leadership work. He manages **Moonstone** (a personality-disordered manipulator he correctly identifies as the team's central threat and central asset), **MACH-1 / Abe Jenkins** (whose redemption he stakes his own credibility on, securing him a presidential pardon in exchange for prison time), **Songbird** (whom he mentors with the same protective intensity he will later show Kate Bishop), and **Atlas, Jolt, and Charcoal**. He runs the team as a probationary program. He extracts compliance through a combination of personal loyalty cultivation and visible self-sacrifice — most notably **trading his own freedom and clean record for MACH-1's pardon**, an act that costs him standing with every other hero in the community.

This period also contains the **Moonstone affair** — a sexual relationship Subject enters with full awareness that Karla Sofen is manipulating him, and which the file should read as the clearest possible confirmation of the attachment pattern. Natasha (KGB), Bobbi (secrets), Moonstone (active manipulation): the women he sleeps with are the women who are *operationally dangerous to him*. The pattern is not unconscious by this point; he has had it pointed out to him by Wanda, by Cap, by Bobbi herself before her death. He continues to enact it. The pattern is older than insight can reach.

## 3.5 Disassembled and the Death

*Avengers Disassembled* (2004) finds Subject back on the East Coast roster, the Thunderbolts behind him. The arc destroys his core. **Jack of Hearts**, recently deceased and reanimated, walks into the mansion grounds. Subject — present, in close proximity, ranged weaponry — is the responder. The explosion that follows takes **Scott Lang's** life and damages Subject's quiver in a way that becomes terminal during the second wave: a Kree assault, Subject's jetpack ignited by enemy fire, the quiver of explosive arrows live on his back. He flies into a Kree ship and detonates.

The death is the most narratively *correct* death he could have died: solitary, sacrificial, unwitnessed in detail, the body unrecovered. **It is also the death the rest of this dossier predicts.** A man whose master motive is *earn the right to be here* will, given the opportunity, pay the bill in full. The file should note: he had time to eject the quiver. He chose not to.

The interval between this death and his restoration in *House of M* (2005) is the only interval in his adult life during which the surveillance file would be, strictly speaking, blank. He is dead for approximately a year of in-universe time. The Avengers disband. The world is rearranged. Cap forms the New Avengers without him.

### 3.5.1 The Resurrection and the Wanda Encounter

He is restored by **Wanda Maximoff** at the climax of *House of M*, off-panel, without ceremony, and without his knowledge of the mechanism. He wakes into a reshaped world. The pursuit of Wanda is the diagnostic event of his post-resurrection year (*New Avengers* #26 / "The Collective" and subsequent material): he tracks her to her hiding place in Wundagore and, in the canonical encounter, **sleeps with her**. The encounter is variously framed in canon — Wanda may not have remembered who he was; he may have intended confrontation and not been able to sustain it; she may have been functionally not herself.

What the file should record without flinching: **the woman who killed him is also the woman who brought him back, and his response to meeting her again is to sleep with her.** The pattern that will not stop. Intimacy and harm predicted by the same cue. The attachment system, presented with the single most overdetermined dangerous-woman cue of his life, does the only thing it knows.

He does not kill her. He cannot. He leaves.

## 3.6 The Ronin Period

He cannot return as Hawkeye. Cap is dead (*Civil War*, 2007). The name belongs to **Kate Bishop** now — a girl from the Upper East Side who picked up the bow and the mantle in the founder's absence (*Young Avengers*, 2005). Subject's first significant act of his post-resurrection life is to *meet Kate, attempt to take the name back, and then give it to her*. The transmission is unscripted, unceremonial, and the moral center of the late period. He gives the girl what no one gave him: the sanction of the senior practitioner, the right to the room.

He takes **Ronin** — a name and identity originally created for Maya Lopez and adopted by Subject at Bendis's pen (*New Avengers* #27, 2007). The symbolism is unsubtle and the file should treat it as deliberate self-description: *the masterless samurai*. Cap is dead. The liege-lord is gone. The name announces the orphaning to anyone who can read it.

Behavioral artifacts of the Ronin period:

- He fights with a sword and grappling tools rather than a bow. The bow is too much him; he needs distance from himself. He is competent with the sword and not elite. The downgrade is the point.
- The mask covers his face entirely. For a man whose career identity was a domino and a smirk, full-face covering is a marked change. He does not want to be recognized. He does not want to be expected to perform Hawkeye. He wants to *do the work without the file*.
- He moves into the Avengers Tower / Sanctum / safehouses as part of Cap's underground New Avengers. Roommates with Spider-Man, Wolverine, Luke Cage, Iron Fist, Spider-Woman (who is, undisclosed to all, a Skrull queen). The file should note: he is once again on a team of damaged people running outside the institution. The Thunderbolts pattern, the WCA pattern, the New Avengers underground pattern — three iterations of the same recruitment logic, only one of which he was nominally in charge of.
- He renews mentorship of **Maya Lopez** during her arc, and begins the long, slow construction of his working relationship with Kate Bishop in parallel. The mentorship register is fully online again; it never goes offline after.

### 3.6.1 Secret Invasion, the Bobbi Return, and the Second Loss

*Secret Invasion* (2008) reveals that the Spider-Woman of the New Avengers was Queen Veranke; that the Avengers' security had been Skrull-compromised at depth for years; and — most personally — that **Bobbi Morse had been Skrull-replaced at the point of her apparent death years earlier, and the real Bobbi has been held captive in a Skrull facility throughout.** She is recovered. She is returned to him.

The file's reading of his response is in section 3.2.2 above and bears restating here: he cannot rebuild. They attempt a reconciliation through the *New Avengers: Reunion* limited (2009) and the *Hawkeye & Mockingbird* ongoing (2010); the reconciliation fails. The grief he had calcified into the structure of his post-WCA identity will not yield up its place to a living woman who is no longer the woman he grieved. The file should be plain: **the second Bobbi is not the first Bobbi, because the first Bobbi is now an internal object he cannot displace.** They part working partners, not spouses. The marriage does not legally end so much as it ends being a marriage.

## 3.7 Dark Reign, Siege, and the Return of the Name

During **Dark Reign** (2009), Norman Osborn assembles his Dark Avengers and gives the Hawkeye identity to **Bullseye**. Subject, still operating as Ronin, sees an assassin wearing his uniform on the news. The file should log this as one of the very small number of moments in canon when his rage runs publicly past his control: he gives a televised interview (*New Avengers* #55) denouncing Osborn by name and announcing the intent to kill him. Cap (now Bucky in the uniform) and the underground team have to physically prevent the assassination attempt that follows.

The diagnostic content: the only thing that has ever drawn Subject into a publicly stated kill-intent is *someone else taking his name*. He can be exiled from the name (Ronin), can give the name to a successor (Kate), can lose every other piece of himself — but the misuse of the name by a murderer is the violation that breaches the wisecrack defense entirely. Identity-as-craft is the one inviolable core; everything else is negotiable.

**Siege** (2010) restores the legitimate order. Cap (Steve) returns to life. Bucky retains the shield for a transitional period; Subject resumes the Hawkeye name and uniform. The Avengers reconstitute. He rejoins the East Coast team as a regular member for the first sustained period in twenty in-universe years.

## 3.8 The Heroic Age Re-Integration and the Quiet Before Fraction

The **Heroic Age** (2010–2012) is the longest stretch of Subject operating *inside* the central institution without satellite escape. He is on the main Avengers roster (Bendis's *Avengers* and *New Avengers* runs), partnered operationally with Spider-Woman, working under Steve Rogers's revived command structure. His function on the team is steady, his behavior on panel is unremarkable, and the file should treat this *as itself a finding*. **He is most depicted as stable in the period immediately before his most depicted depressive episode.** The pattern is consistent with chronic dysthymia masking a building major episode: outward stability concealing internal accrual.

The accrual items, logged across this period:

- **The Bobbi separation** is now permanent.
- **Cap's renewed presence** removes the Ronin justification; he is Hawkeye again, performing the identity he had been allowed to set down.
- **Kate Bishop graduates** from the Young Avengers and begins operating as a peer-level archer; he must now relate to her as a colleague rather than only a successor.
- **Hearing degradation begins again.** The Crossfire-era loss had been variously retconned and healed; ambient cumulative damage from a career of bowstring snap, explosions at proximity, and concussive trauma reasserts itself. He does not seek treatment. The pattern of deferred care is the longest-running medical pattern in the file.
- **The apartment.** He acquires the Bed-Stuy building (off-panel during this stretch, established on-panel in Fraction's run, 2012). The acquisition is funded out of personal accumulation and structured as a tenant-protection move — buying out a slumlord to prevent a tracksuited buyer from evicting the building's working-class residents. The decision is made without consultation with the Avengers, without backup, and without a tactical assessment of the criminal network attached to the property. **He bought a building to save the people in it and inherited a war.** The decision is the single clearest expression in the file of his core operational drive: protect the people the big heroes won't notice. It is also the decision that initiates the next chronological era and ends this one.

---

## 3.9 Patterns Hardened Across the Middle Period

By the end of this stratum the surveillance file should be able to predict, with high accuracy, the behavior of the Subject in any given category:

- **Institutional posture:** will accept satellite leadership; will refuse central leadership; will gravitate to halfway-house team compositions; will treat his own authority as custodial rather than owned.
- **Romantic pattern:** will pursue operationally dangerous women; will not pursue safe ones; will treat the loss of the dangerous ones as confirmation of his fitness for solitude.
- **Stress cycle:** the five-stage humor → withdrawal → stupid risk → exile → return loop, now run end-to-end three times (post-WCA, post-death, post-Skrull-Bobbi) and grooved into the personality as the default response to rupture.
- **Mentorship:** will reliably overinvest in any younger figure framed as a junior self; the controlled version (Songbird, then Kate) is healthy; the uncontrolled version (anyone he reads as a less-fortunate version of his childhood) will become


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# CHRONOLOGY IV — RECENT HISTORY & CURRENT STATE

## 4.1 The Pivot Point: *Civil War II* and the Banner Killing (2016)

The current era of Clinton Francis Barton begins, definitively, in a Utah field with a green-tipped arrow nocked against a draw he has practiced for thirty years. Bruce Banner — alive, ostensibly stable, working on a gamma-suppression protocol — is killed by a single shot through the forehead delivered by the subject. The shot is clean. The subject does not miss. He has never missed a shot he intended to take, and the importance of *intended* in that sentence is the entire psychological architecture of everything that follows.

The pretext is exculpatory: Banner had, months prior, asked the subject to do exactly this if Banner ever lost containment. The subject had agreed. The subject had then modified a custom arrowhead — gamma-inhibitor compound provided by Banner himself — and carried it in his quiver for the intervening period without telling Captain Marvel, the Avengers, S.H.I.E.L.D., or any human being. He carried it the way a man carries a vow.

The trial in Cleveland (*Civil War II* #4, August 2016) acquits him. Steve Rogers testifies. Tony Stark, characteristically, does not forgive him in the moment but signals private comprehension. The video evidence — Banner's own pre-recorded request, played in court — is decisive. The subject sits through the proceeding in a charcoal suit that does not fit at the shoulders (he has not owned a tailored suit since the 1990s; the shoulders are always the problem on him), maintains eye contact with the jury during the verdict, and exits the courthouse without speaking to press. The legal record clears him. The internal record does not, and will not.

**Behavioral residue, observed 2016–present:** the subject has not, since this event, used a broadhead arrow against a sentient target without a documented immediate-threat justification entered into his own private after-action notes. (This documentation pattern was not present pre-2016 and is itself a tell.) He has retired the specific recurve used in Utah; it is stored, unstrung, in a foam-lined case in his Bed-Stuy apartment closet, behind the suit. He does not discuss Banner unless asked directly. When asked, the deflection is verbal but the somatic pattern is consistent: a single hard exhale through the nose, a hand to the back of the neck, and a topic-shift within four to six seconds.

## 4.2 *Occupy Avengers* (2016–2017): The Exile, Productive Variant

Immediately post-acquittal — *immediately*, with luggage still in the courthouse rotunda — the subject leaves New York. This is the fifth or sixth iteration of the **post-event exile pattern** documented in §1, but it is the first iteration in which he routes the exile *toward* work rather than *away* from people. Diagnostically: minor but real progress. The exile is still pathological; the use he makes of it is not.

He spends approximately fourteen months on the road — pickup truck, the older quiver, a duffel, the dog (Lucky, the pizza dog, intermittently shipped between him and Kate Bishop via increasingly elaborate logistical arrangements). The itinerary is granular and reconstructible:

- **Lakota reservation, South Dakota** — water-rights confrontation; partners with Red Wolf (Will Talltrees); first sustained collaboration with a Native hero in his career; sleeps in the truck bed three weeks running.
- **Gulf Coast** — hurricane-displaced communities; works alongside the Night Nurse network; develops the first depicted Spanish-language competence in his canon (rudimentary, kitchen-Spanish, but present and used).
- **Appalachia, eastern Kentucky** — meta-human pollution incident; partners briefly with a then-unaffiliated Wonder Man.
- **Border country, Arizona/Sonora** — declines a Department of Homeland Security request to interdict crossings; the refusal is on the record and is the cleanest articulation in his career of his operational code re: institutions versus persons.

He returns to New York in late 2017 thinner by roughly twelve pounds, with a new scar along the left forearm (knife, not arrow), and with a small notebook of names and addresses — civilians he has promised to check on — that he will, atypically for him, actually check on over the following three years.

## 4.3 *Tales of Suspense* (2017–2018): The Bucky Interlude

The Winter Soldier is presumed dead. The subject does not believe it.
