The under-reaction is itself the reaction — Crossfire is too important to take seriously, because taking him seriously would require Subject to sit with what was lost.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure Mode.** Auditory-domain ambushes. Subject has gotten better at managing this; not all the way.

**Leverage.** Crossfire's primary weapon is the threat of repetition. Subject has accepted, in advance, that he may lose more of his hearing on any given mission. The acceptance is part of how he keeps going.

---

#### Trick Shot (Buck Chisholm)
*First sustained adult contact:* Solo Avengers #1–2 (1987), and recurring.

**History.** Childhood mentor. Taught him the bow. Beat him. Abandoned him. Returned years later to try to kill him as a contract job and, later still, dying of cancer, asked him for a final reckoning.

**Dynamics.** This is the enemy with whom the line between mentor and abuser is most visibly drawn in marker. Every encounter is a re-litigation of the childhood. Subject cannot kill him and cannot forgive him and cannot stop showing up when called.

**Trust / Safety.** Trust: zero. Safety: emotional — none. Physical — Subject is now better than him and both know it, which is its own torsion.

**Failure Mode.** Subject hesitates. He has hesitated on the draw against Trick Shot in ways he does not hesitate against any other shooter, and the hesitation is the childhood reaching up through the floorboards.

**Leverage.** Trick Shot can summon him with a phone call. The leverage is total within its small domain and zero outside it.

---

#### Swordsman (Jacques Duquesne)
*First adult contact:* Avengers #19 (1965); recurrent through the Mantis-era Avengers and beyond.

**History.** The other childhood mentor; the one who beat him nearly to death for catching him stealing. Reappears as Avengers villain, then as Avengers member seeking redemption, then dies redemptively (Giant-Size Avengers #2, 1974). Has been resurrected and reconfigured in subsequent decades, always with the same emotional valence: a man who failed Subject and seeks, fitfully, to undo it.

**Dynamics.** Subject is more able to grant Swordsman partial absolution than Trick Shot because Swordsman, in the end, died well. The redemption arc gave Subject's internal court a verdict it could enter. Trick Shot has never given him that, and the asymmetry tells.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A in current continuity.

**Failure Mode.** Historical.

**Leverage.** Posthumous. Subject's willingness to take in redemption-seeking former villains owes a great deal to this casefile.

---

#### Barney Barton
The brother is mapped in Relationships II as family rather than enemy, but he belongs in this section by function as much as in that one by blood. He is the original enemy — the protector who became the betrayer, the betrayer who keeps almost becoming the brother again. Every adversarial relationship in this section is, in some part, a re-staging of him. Subject's inability to definitively close the file on Barney is the reason he cannot definitively close any of the others.

---

#### The Clown (Kazimierz Kazimierczak), of the Tracksuit Draculas era
*First contact:* Fraction's Hawkeye run (2012–2015).

**History.** A circus-trained assassin sent by the Bed-Stuy mob bosses to kill Subject in his own apartment building. The choice of operator is not coincidence; the building's antagonist sent a carnival-killer at a carnival-kid, and the symmetry was deliberate. The Clown nearly succeeded — shot Subject in the head, took most of his already-compromised hearing, killed the dog (briefly, narratively), wrecked the apartment.

**Dynamics.** This is the enemy who made the Fraction-era reckoning happen. He represents, in costume, every part of Subject's past Subject thought he had outrun. The fight is therefore not a fight in the ordinary sense; it is an exorcism that Subject only partly wins.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure Mode.** Subject took the hit because he insisted on standing his ground in a civilian building with his neighbors in it — the *protect-the-people-the-big-heroes-won't* drive overriding tactical sense, exactly as the personality profile predicts.

**Leverage.** The Clown is gone, in current continuity, but the template he represents — *the past in greasepaint* — is recurrent, and Subject is reliably destabilized by anyone who walks in wearing it.

---

#### The Tracksuit Draculas (organizational)
*First contact:* as above.

A collective adversary worth its own line because the structural relationship is novel in his casefile. They are not super-villains; they are landlords with hired thugs. Subject's willingness to wage a months-long private war against a mid-tier organized-crime outfit over the housing rights of strangers is the cleanest single piece of evidence in the published record for the priority-two drive in the personality profile (*protect the people the big heroes won't notice*). He took a near-fatal head wound and lost the remainder of his hearing for an apartment building. He would do it again tomorrow. They know this; it is, in the end, why they lost.

---

#### General-Category Enemies: the Brotherhood, Hydra, AIM, the Hand, assorted cosmic intrusions
Subject's relationship to organizational enemies is professional and largely affectless. He fights them well; he does not invest. The investment is reserved for personal adversaries — the mentors, the brother, the Clown, the Osborn — because those are the ones who map onto the internal substrate. Faceless evil is, paradoxically, a relief: he can fight it without it touching him. This is itself a piece of self-care, and one of the few he reliably permits himself.

---

### CROSS-CATEGORY SYNTHESIS

Three patterns recur across the rivals/enemies/lovers spectrum and warrant flagging:

1. **The handler-lover template.** Romanoff installed it; every subsequent lover has been measured against it; the rivals he respects are the ones who can play handler (Cap, never in this section, but referenced) and the enemies who can play handler-imposter (Osborn, the Clown) are the ones who get under his armor.

2. **The mentor-betrayer template.** Trick Shot and Swordsman installed it; every authority figure Subject encounters is screened against it; rivals who feel like mentors-who-might-betray (Stark, Walker in a different key) generate the friction he calls rivalry, while enemies who explicitly *are* betraying mentors (Trick Shot himself, recurrently) generate the hesitation he cannot train out.

3. **The carnival-mirror.** Bullseye, the Clown, Trick Shot, Swordsman, and, in his way, Barney — all are *Clint Barton with a piece broken or missing*. Subject fights better against people who are nothing like him and worse against people who are almost him, because in the latter case half of him is fighting on the other side of the line. The published record contains no enemy who is wholly unlike him whom he has not eventually beaten, and no enemy who is mostly like him whom he has beaten cleanly. The pattern will hold. He will keep entering those fights anyway. He does not know how not to.

— END SECTION 6 —


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER: BARTON, CLINTON FRANCIS
## Section VI — Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

### Preamble: The Diagnostic Significance of Powerlessness

Before any inventory, the operative fact: the subject has **no superhuman capacity of any kind**. No mutation, no augmentation, no exposure-event, no surgical or chemical enhancement, no mystical conferral, no symbiotic passenger, no armor system, no inherited gift. Every capability listed below is the product of approximately three decades (in-universe) of unrelieved, conscious, physically punishing rehearsal — and the **fact that he chose this life and has refused, on multiple documented occasions, the opportunity to upgrade himself out of it** is the first and most important data point in this section. Pym offered. Stark offered. SHIELD repeatedly offered. He declined each time. The chosen identity of "the guy with the bow" — see Section 3 on counter-dependence and earned legitimacy — is enforced by an active and ongoing refusal of power. He requires his abilities to remain those a determined human could in principle replicate, because the entire psychological architecture of his life depends on his presence on the team being **earned and not gifted**.

This makes the surveillance picture unusually clean. There is no power-set whose ceiling is unknown. There is a man, a bow, a quiver, and roughly forty years of muscle memory. Everything else is technique, calibration, and improvisation.

---

### 1. The Primary Skill: Archery

**Mechanism.** Conventional Olympic-recurve and compound mechanics, refined far beyond Olympic specification. Draw weights observed in canon between 150 and 250 lbs on combat bows — well above the 50–60 lb range of competition archery and at the upper bound of historical English warbow loads. Sustained accuracy at these poundages over a fight that may run minutes implies posterior chain, scapular stabilizer, and forearm-flexor development of a magnitude only achievable by daily drawing over decades. He warms up before missions when he can; he can fight cold when he cannot, and pays for it in next-day shoulder pain that is depicted in panel (Fraction's run, repeated background beats: ice on the right shoulder, the rotator-cuff stretch in the kitchen, the wince when reaching for a high cabinet).

**Range.** Observed accurate shots from contact distance (point-blank, into a grappling assailant) to depicted hits beyond a half-mile under favorable conditions. Functional precision envelope: **head-sized target at 300 yards in calm air with a familiar bow** is the floor of his normal operational reliability. Beyond 400 yards he becomes a probability event rather than a certainty — still better than any other human shooter on the planet, but his own internal standard treats anything over 500 yards as a "lucky" shot, and he tells the truth about which ones were.

**Accuracy Specification.** Across canon he has performed: arrow-splitting (Robin-Hood-style, multiple instances), shooting through the eye-slits of moving helmeted opponents, deflecting incoming projectiles by shooting them mid-air, threading shots through narrow gaps (window blinds, between hostages and captors at <12-inch margins), trick shots off curved surfaces, blind shots calibrated by sound, ricochets calculated three and four cushions deep. The signature trick, **shooting the bowstring of another archer mid-draw**, is a real recurring move and not a writer's flourish — he uses it as a calling card against opponents who challenge him on his own ground (Trick Shot, Bullseye on at least one occasion).

**Rate of Fire.** Demonstrated nock-draw-release cycles in the 0.6–0.9 second range for unaimed suppression and the 1.5–2.5 second range for aimed precision shots. Multi-arrow draws (two and three arrows simultaneously, fanned releases) appear when he is outnumbered, with predictably reduced per-shot accuracy. He has been depicted firing approximately a dozen arrows in under ten seconds during high-pressure engagements; whether the body actually sustains this is left to the artist, but the canonical fact is that he can empty a quiver faster than most opponents can close the distance.

**Off-Hand.** Documented capable with both hands. Prefers right; will switch to left when the right is injured or when geometry demands it, with a tolerable accuracy penalty (perhaps 15–20% by his own estimation). The fact that he trained the off-hand at all is a tell about his risk-modeling: he expected to be wounded and prepared for it.

---

### 2. Trick Arrows: Inventory, Doctrine, Logistics

The quiver is a tactical toolkit, not an ornament. He builds his own; this is canon-consistent across runs and not delegated to Stark Industries except in specific arc circumstances. He is, by hand and by training, a **competent applied engineer at the level of a senior special-effects technician or a sophisticated bomb-maker**, with metallurgy, basic electronics, propellant chemistry, and pneumatic-mechanical fabrication all within his working range. He cannot build an Iron Man suit; he can build, has built, and routinely repairs every arrow he carries.

**Categorized inventory** (drawn from forty years of depicted use):

- *Kinetic / penetration:* broadhead, bodkin, armor-piercing tungsten-tip, blunt-impact (boxing-glove and rubber-tip variants for non-lethal takedowns).
- *Explosive:* concussion, fragmentation, demolition (shaped-charge), flash-bang. The flash-bang is the most used arrow in his quiver by a wide margin; he treats it as the universal solvent of bad situations.
- *Chemical:* gas (sleep, tear, smoke, marker dye), acid, adhesive.
- *Electrical:* taser-tip, EMP, electromagnet.
- *Mechanical-deployment:* net, bola, grapple-and-line (his secondary mobility system, see below), cable-cutter, suction-cup, magnetic-anchor.
- *Sensor / utility:* tracer (radio-emitting), camera/microphone, flare, signal-flare, putty.
- *Specialty / situational:* sonic (responsible for his original hearing loss — see Medical), thermite, cryo, the deeply embarrassing "Pym-particle-tipped" antique used to deliver a shrunken Ant-Man across distance, and the genuinely strange one-offs (the "USB" arrow in modern runs, the boomerang-arrow, etc.).

**Doctrine.** He standardizes color-coding on the fletching and on the shaft band so that he can identify by feel in low-light or with eyes-on-target. Quiver organization is **mirrored left-to-right by use-frequency**, with flash-bangs and broadheads at the dominant-hand draw position and specialty arrows packed deeper. This is a logistical habit borrowed from carnival staging — props placed where the hand goes without looking. He restocks compulsively after every engagement, often the same night, and the act of fletching is depicted as one of the few sources of genuine calm in his life. Note the symptom-as-signature reading: **the man who cannot sit still with himself can sit for hours making the next batch of arrows.** Repetitive craft as self-regulation.

**Logistics & limits.** Quiver capacity is depicted variably (18–30 arrows standard, larger backpack-rig in heavy-combat scenarios). Once empty, he is — by his own framing — "just a guy." This is the load-out constraint that drives most of his tactical decision-making: **every shot must be priced against the remaining quiver state**, and he triages aggressively. The "did I really need an exploding arrow for this guy" gag is operational doctrine, not just humor.

---

### 3. Secondary Weapon Skills

The bow is the signature; the actual armory is broader.

- **Thrown weapons.** Knives, shuriken, improvised objects (coins, billiard balls, silverware, his own arrows held in the hand and thrown). His hand-eye system generalizes — accuracy with anything he can grip is **substantially above special-forces baseline**, and he uses this fact in confined spaces where a bow is unworkable.
- **Firearms.** Trained, competent, contemptuous. Will use a gun when the situation demands it; visibly dislikes doing so. Pistol and long-arm certification is presumed from his SHIELD reserve status; sniper-rifle accuracy is depicted as excellent but he treats the rifle as inferior to a good bow at sub-800-yard ranges because the bow is silent, recoverable, and his.
- **Sword and staff.** Competent, not expert. The Swordsman trained him in fencing before the betrayal; he retains the fundamentals and has handled a sword in canon under duress. He prefers staff/improvised blunt weapons (escrima, single stick) for melee, and the bow itself doubles as a club at close range.
- **Hand-to-hand.** Trained across multiple Avengers and SHIELD curricula — Captain America gave him the boxing fundamentals personally, Romanoff gave him systema-influenced close-quarters work, Iron Fist and Daredevil have both sparred with him. His personal style is **a brawler's frame with technical inserts**: he fights like a midwestern bar fighter who has been to graduate school in violence. Will take a punch to land one. Prefers economy-of-motion takedowns when he has the option and ugly grappling when he does not. Honest assessment: a top-tier non-powered hand-to-hand combatant, behind Cap, Natasha, Bucky, Danny Rand, and the Murdock tier, ahead of essentially everyone else.

---

### 4. Acrobatics, Mobility, and the Carnival Substrate

The carnival training is the under-cited foundation of half his combat capability. He was tumbling, wire-walking, and trapeze-rigging before he ever drew a bow seriously, and the proprioceptive and balance training shows in every fight scene.

- **Acrobatic baseline.** Olympic-gymnast level tumbling, comfortable working at height without protection, accurate jumps to small targets, depicted firing accurately while in mid-air or while falling. He retains this into late career despite the accumulated joint damage; it is the skill set he refuses to let go of even when his body has begun to argue.
- **Grapple-and-line mobility.** The grapple arrow is his Spider-Man swing-line analog and is used as such in dense urban terrain. He is genuinely good at it; he is also depicted missing the catch on at least three occasions across canon and falling, which is part of the running joke that has a survival-data floor underneath it: **his mobility system fails roughly 5–10% of the time, and he has trained to fall well.**
- **Free-running / parkour.** Standard Avengers vocabulary, executed at the high end of unenhanced human capability. He chooses ground routes through a building the way a chess player chooses an opening, and his ability to **pre-visualize a route through a hostile space** before entering it is one of his quieter elite skills.

---

### 5. Sensory and Perceptual Capabilities

This is the area where his "non-powered" status is most strained, because the published evidence shows perceptual ability at the upper bound of human possibility — possibly past it.

- **Visual acuity.** Repeatedly depicted as significantly above 20/20; some writers have put him at 20/10 or better. He picks out details at distances most people would need optics for. Whether this is a quiet writer-given enhancement or simply elite-shooter visual training is left ambiguous; the surveillance read is **trained acuity at the absolute ceiling of unaugmented human capacity**, not a true superhuman gift.
- **Range estimation.** Sub-conscious and near-instant. He reads distance, wind, drop, and target velocity the way a fluent speaker reads a sentence, without breaking it into parts. This is the **single most automated cognitive skill he possesses** and it survives intoxication, exhaustion, head trauma, and emotional collapse.
- **Spatial pattern recognition.** He sees ricochet lines, sight-lines, and trajectory geometry that other operators have to compute. The "shot map" of a room — what angles are available, what cover is real versus apparent, which surfaces will deflect cleanly — assembles itself in his head on entry.
- **Auditory compensation.** Post-hearing-loss, his use of vibration, visual scanning, and spatial pre-mapping has expanded to compensate. He reads lips fluently, signs fluently (the *Hawkeye* #19 issue establishes this on the page), and tracks footfall vibration through floors. The disability is real; the workarounds are elite.

---

### 6. Cognitive and Tactical Capabilities

The "dumb archer" framing addressed in Section 3 is a self-protective mask. The operational record shows:

- **Tactical command.** Founded and ran the West Coast Avengers for five years. Recruited, vetted, trained, deployed. Managed Vision, Wanda, Wonder Man, Tigra, USAgent — not a collection of stable people — and held the unit together through that entire period. This is mid-to-senior O-grade command competence, exercised under conditions no actual military officer would accept.
- **Improvised problem-solving.** His signature cognitive mode. Give him a room, thirty seconds, and an objective, and he will produce a plan that uses three things in the room nobody else noticed were weapons. This is the carnival kid plus the trick-shot training plus the chronic underdog mentality — *the only way out is the way nobody is watching*.
- **Strategic intelligence.** Lower than his tactical. He is a brilliant chess player on a single board and a mediocre one across the whole tournament. Long-horizon planning is not where he lives; it is part of why he follows Cap rather than competing with him.
- **Languages.** English native; functional Russian (Natasha era), conversational ASL (now primary), fragments of several others picked up in fieldwork. Not a polyglot, but not the monoglot the framing implies.
- **Mechanical and electronic aptitude.** See above on arrow fabrication. He maintains his own equipment top to bottom, repairs vehicles, picks locks competently, defeats basic electronic security, can wire an explosive. He cannot do what Stark does; he can do what a senior SHIELD field engineer does.

---

### 7. Growth Curve Over the Longitudinal Record

The diagnostic finding: **the skill ceiling plateaued early and the floor has slowly risen.**

Peak archery accuracy was reached within five years of his Avengers debut and has not meaningfully improved since — there is no room above where he started. What has changed across forty years is **judgment, restraint, economy, and recovery**. Early Hawkeye over-shot, over-talked, and over-committed. Mature Hawkeye fires fewer arrows, picks better ones, and ends fights sooner. The tactical IQ rose through the West Coast Avengers period and has held steady since. Hand-to-hand has gradually improved through sustained exposure to higher-tier sparring partners. The body has slowly declined; the technique has compensated.

The acute change-points are: the hearing loss (forced compensatory development of every other sensory and signaling channel), the death-and-return (no observable skill change, significant observable affect change), the Bobbi loss (introduced a permanent fractional hesitation in fights where civilians are at stake), and the Banner killing (introduced an additional and more dangerous hesitation about lethal shots against targets who could be argued to want it). Each loss adds a small disabling clause to his combat code, and he accepts each clause as the price.

---

### 8. The Hard Limits

A complete file requires the negative inventory.

- **No augmentation.** Vulnerable to anything that bypasses skill: poison, disease, ambush, the lucky bullet, the unlucky fall. He has been knocked out by ordinary head blows on multiple occasions.
- **Ammunition-dependent.** Out of arrows, he is a top-tier human and nothing more. He has fought his way out of empty-quiver situations more times than is statistically plausible, and he knows this; the post-engagement re-stocking ritual is part-superstition.
- **Line-of-sight-dependent.** Cannot shoot what he cannot see or precisely infer. Heavy smoke, total darkness, and certain illusion/perception powers shut him down. He has compensated with sound-tracking and trained blind shots, but the compensation is partial.
- **Vulnerable at extreme close range.** A grappler inside his draw distance neutralizes the bow. He knows this; he trains for it; he still prefers not to be there.
- **Body is finite.** The shoulder, the back, the knees, the hearing, the cumulative concussions. He fights at a level his physiology can no longer fully underwrite, and the long-term trajectory is downward regardless of will.
- **Cannot withstand sustained psychic, magical, or technological mind-control.** Has been compromised by Crossfire's sonics, by Loki, by various others. Hates this category of attack with a personal heat that exceeds the tactical threat — see Section 3 on agency.

---

### 9. Symptom-as-Signature Reading

The skill set is the psychology in physical form, and the correspondences are exact.

**He chose a weapon that requires patience, silence, and solitude** — and that demands a fresh decision for every single shot. There is no spray-and-pray with a bow. Every arrow is an individually authored act of violence, recoverable if it misses, irrecoverable if it lands. This is the weapon of a man whose conscience requires him to **own each act of harm separately**. A rifle would let him kill at volume; he does not want that permission.

**The trick arrows are improvised parenting of a chaotic world.** Each one is a problem pre-solved, a contingency pre-loved-into-being by a man who was given no contingencies in his own childhood. The compulsive fletching is restitution: he makes for others, in advance, the prepared safety nobody made for him.

**The carnival acrobatics are the joy substrate.** Watch him in motion in a well-drawn fight — the tumbling, the impossible angles, the showmanship. This is the only place his childhood gave him something good, and he has never stopped using it. The carnival made him; the carnival also kept the part of him that can still be delighted.

**The refusal of augmentation is the refusal of forgiveness.** To accept a power upgrade would be to accept that he deserves to be on the team without continuing to earn it. He cannot have that. The bow keeps the debt active. The bow keeps him in the position of having to show up tomorrow and prove it again.

**The hearing loss tells the whole story in miniature.** The first major injury of his career was self-inflicted, in a desperate improvisation that saved someone else, at the cost of a sense he then refused to treat for decades. The pattern of his entire life is in that single act: **damage himself to fix the immediate problem, defer the cost, walk it off, joke about it, return to work.** The arrows are how he does his job. The deafness is how he does his life.

He is, at the end of the inventory, **a man who has built an elite combat capability entirely out of refusals** — refused to be his father, refused to be his brother, refused to be augmented, refused to be the kind of hero who kills at scale, refused to be treated, refused to be helped, refused to leave the work. The capability is real and is also a monument to what he will not allow himself to become. The bow is the visible part. The refusals are the structure.


## Operational Profile & Tradecraft

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER: BARTON, CLINTON FRANCIS
## Section 6 — Operational Profile & Tradecraft

---

### 6.1 Decision Architecture

Subject's decision-making is best modeled as a **two-clock system** running at incompatible speeds.

The **fast clock** is exceptional. Under live combat load — kinetic, time-compressed, ballistic — Barton resolves complex multi-variable problems in well under a second. The signature canonical demonstration is the running ballistic computation he is shown to perform mid-engagement: range, wind, target velocity, arrow weight, draw torque, his own pulse and respiration phase, the trajectory of teammates' bodies through his shooting lanes. He has, on the record, threaded shots between two moving allies into a third moving hostile (*West Coast Avengers* run, repeatedly; *Avengers* vol. 3 under Busiek; the Ultron engagements). He plans his shots two and three arrows ahead, in the manner of a billiards player setting the cue ball — selecting not just the shot but the *next* shot's geometry. This is procedural cognition operating at near-autonomic speed and is the single most underestimated thing about him.

The **slow clock** — strategic planning at the hours-to-days scale, particularly when the variables include his own emotional state or interpersonal stakes — is markedly worse. Here Barton substitutes **action for analysis.** He goes, then thinks. The Fraction run is a chronicle of decisions made by walking into rooms; *Hawkeye: Freefall* is a chronicle of decisions made by leaving them. When asked to articulate a plan in advance, he tends to produce a half-plan he intends to improvise around, and he is aware of this and presents it as a virtue. ("I had a plan. The plan was 'shoot the guy.' The plan worked.") The deficit is not intelligence — see §3 — but **affective interference with deliberation.** Slowing down forces him into proximity with the parts of himself he does not want to inspect, so he does not slow down.

The **operational consequence**: Barton is a superb tactician and a poor strategist of his own life, and the same trait — bias toward immediate action — is the mechanism of both. Handlers and team leaders who give him *the next forty-five seconds* get the best operator in the field. Handlers who give him *the next six weeks* get a man who will solve it in week one by doing something reckless and spend weeks two through six fixing the consequences.

---

### 6.2 Primary Weapon System and Craft

The bow is not a weapon; it is a **prosthetic conscience.** Every arrow he looses is, by mechanical fact, a decision he has made with his own hand and held in tension for the duration of the draw. He cannot fire and forget. The weapon enforces deliberation in the one domain where his deliberation is reliable, and this is part of why he has stayed with it through six decades of canonical mockery about its obsolescence.

**Equipment baseline:**

- **Bows:** subject rotates among multiple custom recurve and compound builds, draw weights consistently depicted in the 250 lb range for his "heavy" battle bow (a figure outside human norms and best understood as Marvel-physics shorthand for peak-human upper-body conditioning sustained over decades). A lighter ~125 lb travel bow is his default urban carry. He keeps a takedown bow in a hard case sized to civilian luggage and has been shown assembling it on rooftops, in elevators, and once mid-fall.
- **Quiver:** trick-arrow loadout varies by mission. Standardized inventory includes blunt-impact, putty, EMP, acid, suction-cup grapple, explosive, smoke, flare, net, sonic, boomerang, acoustic-decoy, and a recurring "regular pointy" type he labels with what amounts to deadpan professional pride. Documented totals run 30+ distinct heads at peak loadout. He fletches and assembles most of these himself; the only exceptions are the EMP and explosive heads, which are sourced through SHIELD/Avengers quartermaster channels and which he treats with markedly more respect than the rest.
- **Sidearms:** subject is competent with firearms but uses them rarely and reluctantly; canonical instances are stress-marked (the Banner shot was a rifle, not a bow — diagnostically meaningful: he chose the weapon that distanced him from the decision rather than the one that forced him to own it).
- **Edged weapons:** Swordsman trained him; he carries a knife but does not romance it. The Ronin-period sword work was competent but visibly secondary to his bow craft.
- **Vehicles:** Sky-Cycle (West Coast era — modified Quinjet auxiliary), a long history of nondescript Detroit-iron sedans and pickup trucks chosen for the same reasons surveillance professionals choose them (parts availability, visual invisibility, mechanically simple). He drives well, fast, and aggressively, and he can hot-wire pre-2000 American makes from memory. He prefers manual transmissions because the shifter gives him something to do with his hand.

**Skill stack beyond archery:** acrobatics at carnival-pro level; pickpocketing and sleight-of-hand (carnival residue, still operational, used recurrently for lifting keys, wallets, and badges); lock work at intermediate-pro level; basic field demolitions; passable trauma medicine on himself and others; fluent ASL (post-Fraction); functional Russian (the Widow years, the tracksuit-mob years); workable conversational Spanish; reads Italian and a little Latin from a long-running interest he downplays. He plays the guitar badly and the harmonica creditably.

---

### 6.3 Operational Tempo and Tradecraft

Barton's tradecraft is a **hybrid of three traditions** that do not normally cohabit a single operator: carnival huckster, Avengers cape, and journeyman SHIELD field agent. The result is an idiosyncratic kit that is hard to model and harder to interdict.

**Pre-operational behaviors.** He scouts. He genuinely scouts, on foot, in person, with eyes — not just sat imagery and dossiers. He walks the perimeter of a target site in worn boots and a hooded sweatshirt, eats at the diner across the street, tips the waitress in cash, and gets the names of the regulars. The carnival kid surveils the way a midway barker reads a crowd: who has money, who is armed, who is drunk, who is bored, who is watching whom. He notes exits compulsively and can produce, from any room he has been in for more than ninety seconds, an accurate count of doors, windows, sight-lines, improvised weapons, and load-bearing structural members. This is not a parlor trick; it is a continuous background process he cannot turn off.

**Time-on-target.** He prefers short engagements with prepared egress. The Fraction-era apartment defense is anomalous in his record precisely because it is a *fixed-position* fight, and he loses it repeatedly. His historical record favors mobility: enter from elevation, engage from concealment, exfiltrate before the opposition can localize him. The bow is selected for this — it is silent on release, the projectile is recoverable, and unlike a firearm it does not announce his position with muzzle flash or report.

**Communication discipline.** Inconsistent and a known liability. He breaks comms protocol when he is amused, frightened, or trying to manage someone else's panic — usually with a joke that gives away tactically irrelevant information that is nonetheless logged by anyone listening. He has been called on this by every team leader he has served under. He does not stop. The chatter is, again, **affect management**; silence under stress takes him to places he cannot operate from.

**Cover and concealment.** Bad at sustained false-identity cover; good at situational disappearance. He is too restless and too forthright to maintain a deep legend over months — he will break cover to defend a stranger from being shortchanged at a bodega. But he can vanish into a city for weeks at a time using nothing more sophisticated than a different jacket, a baseball cap, and the kind of physical unobtrusiveness that working-class men in their forties wear by default. The purple costume is an *operational choice he has examined and kept;* when he wants to be seen he wants to be seen *loud,* because loud Hawkeye draws fire away from civilians and onto the only person on the rooftop wearing body conditioning that can take it.

**Counter-surveillance.** Solid baseline. He runs SDRs (surveillance detection routes) reflexively when moving to anything he considers a safe location, varies his routes home, has a noted habit of doubling back through bodegas and pizza places (which double as informal intel nodes — he knows the counter staff). He sweeps his apartment for bugs irregularly and badly; he relies more on the assumption that he is *probably* bugged than on confirmation, which is, perversely, the correct posture.

**HUMINT.** This is a strength insufficiently noted. Barton runs a personal network of low-level civilian informants that no formal apparatus could replicate: the super of the building across from his, the dispatcher at a 24-hour diner, two specific subway buskers, a retired cop on his block, a teenager who works at a comic shop, the woman who runs the laundromat. He pays them in respect, small cash, and reciprocal favors. None of them would call themselves his assets. All of them tell him things. The carnival taught him how to talk to people who do not normally get talked to, and he has never stopped doing it.

---

### 6.4 Infrastructure and Safehouses

The subject's infrastructure footprint is **deliberately small, deliberately distributed, and deliberately cheap.** He does not maintain a Stark-tier base. He maintains a constellation of cheap rooms.

**Primary domicile (rotating).** The Bed-Stuy apartment building (227 Front Street/equivalent depending on continuity) was both home and ward — he owned it, lived in it, and defended it, the three roles deliberately collapsed. Subsequent residences have followed a pattern: rent-stabilized walk-ups, low floors near fire escapes, units with sight-lines to at least one major artery. He prefers buildings with elderly tenants and noisy radiators; both serve as ambient surveillance against intrusion.

**Safehouses.** At least five known, likely more, scattered across the Five Boroughs, Iowa, the Pacific Northwest, and a recurring storage unit in New Jersey that has appeared across multiple writers. None are luxurious. Each contains, at minimum: a takedown bow, a quiver of basic loadout, $2,000–$5,000 in mixed small bills, two changes of clothes, a burner phone, a first-aid kit weighted toward bullet and burn care, a sleeping bag, instant coffee. The storage unit is the **arsenal**, holding bulk-purchased arrow shafts, fletching, broadhead stock, the gear he does not want associated with any of his addresses.

**Workshop.** Wherever he is currently living, the kitchen table is the workshop. He fletches arrows watching television. He sharpens and reseats broadheads at the diner counter with the staff's tolerance. He has been depicted disassembling and oiling a bow grip during Avengers briefings — which reads as inattention and is in fact a self-soothing focal task that keeps him present in the room.

**The Avengers as infrastructure.** He uses Avengers/Stark resources for what they are uniquely good at (quinjet transport, comms, satellite, the heavy munitions arrows) and refuses them for what he can do himself (lodging, vehicles, day-to-day operations). The refusal is partly principled and partly **counter-dependence per §3** — he will not owe Tony Stark the rent.

---

### 6.5 Finances

A near-empty ledger maintained with the discipline of someone who is one bad month from broke and has been since he was a child.

**Income streams (documented or strongly implied):** Avengers stipend (intermittent, sometimes refused on principle); SHIELD consultancy fees (back-channel, when active); occasional security/bodyguard work; the rental income from the Bed-Stuy building, which he immediately spent back into the building's maintenance and his tenants' arrears; residual carnival-era cash from a series of small, off-books prize-fighting and trick-shot exhibitions earlier in life that the record hints at without detailing. No inheritance. No investments. No retirement account.

**Outflows:** rent (his own and, repeatedly, other people's); arrow components in bulk; coffee; pizza; veterinary care for the dog; cash gifts to former carnival contacts who have hit hard times; legal fees that recur every time Barney resurfaces. He pays his bills late and in full. He has never declared bankruptcy and has come within a payment of it more than once.

**Cash discipline.** Mid-three-figures in mixed bills on his person at all times, distributed across at least three locations on his body (boot, jacket lining, wallet). The wallet itself is a decoy as much as a wallet — it contains enough cash to satisfy a mugger and a thoroughly unremarkable set of cards. The real money is in the boot. Carnival rule.

**Credit posture.** Poor by civilian standards, irrelevant by operational ones. He pays in cash because cash does not leave a trail and because he does not trust his future self to make the payments. The latter is the more honest reason and he knows it.

---

### 6.6 Information Discipline

**Digital footprint.** Minimal and deliberately so. He owns smartphones — usually a series of cheap prepaid burners alongside one Stark-issued or SHIELD-issued device that he keeps in a Faraday pouch when not in active use. He texts more than he calls (post-hearing-loss adaptation that long preceded the hearing aids). His texting style is bluntly diagnostic: lower-case, terse, unpunctuated, generous with the period as a stop-marker when he is angry. He uses one social media account, sporadically, badly, and has been counseled to stop multiple times.

**Documentation.** He keeps physical notebooks. Cheap composition books, one running at a time, used as a combined operations log, tactical sketch pad, grocery list, and informal journal. He sketches sight-lines, fletching variations, arrow-head schematics. The journal entries are minimal and oblique — never names, never specifics, but enough to date-anchor his memory of an event. He burns old notebooks when they fill. Investigators recovering one would get tactical intelligence and almost no biographical leverage, which is exactly the design.

**OPSEC tells against him.** He photographs his dog. He calls Kate Bishop on her actual phone. He sends Captain America postcards. These are deliberate, willed breaches of his own discipline because total OPSEC would require severing the relationships that are keeping him alive, and he has, somewhere, made that trade consciously.

---

### 6.7 Signature Operational Tells

Items here are pattern-matchable across decades of canonical sightings and would constitute, in a surveillance context, a high-confidence behavioral fingerprint.

1. **The rooftop pre-engagement ritual.** On overwatch, he removes his right glove, blows on the fingers regardless of weather, replaces it, then nocks. The glove-off-glove-on serves no mechanical purpose; it is a self-cue he developed as a child performer to mark the transition from preparation to performance.
2. **Three-arrow draw.** When anticipating a multi-target engagement, he draws two additional arrows from the quiver and holds them in the bow hand against the riser, ready to nock. This is a circus-trick-shot habit, not standard archery practice, and it is unique to him in the field.
3. **The over-shoulder shot.** He will, given any excuse, take at least one shot per engagement that requires him to fire backward over his own shoulder or off a reflective surface. Witnesses read it as showboating. It is partly showboating and partly a continuous self-test: *am I still the guy who can make this shot.* The day he stops trying it is the day to worry.
4. **Verbal tic in pre-engagement.** "Okay" — repeated, two to four times, softly, to himself, as he ranges the target. Spotters who know him have used this as a fire-imminent cue.
5. **Post-engagement arrow recovery.** He retrieves his arrows. Always. Even under fire, even when the tactical situation argues against it. Three reasons, in descending order of how willing he is to articulate them: cost, OPSEC (arrows are signature evidence), and a deep-seated refusal to leave his tools behind that has carnival origins (the kid who lost a knife was the kid who got beaten).
6. **The coffee thing.** He drinks coffee constantly, badly, prefers it bitter and black, and the cup is in his hand more often than not when he is on standby. Removing the cup from his hand is a reliable indicator that he has shifted from passive to active alert.
7. **Hearing-aid management.** Post-Fraction era, he removes his hearing aids before known explosive engagements, before sleep, and — diagnostically — before conversations he does not want to have, with plausible deniability. The last category is the tell.
8. **Left-side bias.** He stands to the left of doorways, sits to the left of tables, lets allies pass on his right. This was originally a dominant-eye/draw-side adjustment and has hardened into reflex. Investigators looking for him in CCTV should weight left-side body positioning.
9. **Civilian intervention reflex.** He cannot walk past a street-level injustice — a mugging, a domestic, a kid being shaken down — without engaging. This is a near-total override of operational discipline and has compromised covert movements on multiple occasions. Anyone running him as an asset accounts for it; anyone hunting him exploits it.
10. **The dog.** Wherever Lucky the dog is, Barton is within a 30-minute return radius, or believes he is. The animal is both companion and unintended tracking beacon.
11. **Self-deprecation cadence.** When the jokes get tighter, faster, and more anatomically specific about his own incompetence, he is in psychological extremis. Quiet Hawkeye is concerning. *Funny* Hawkeye is the loudest distress signal he emits.
12. **The exile signature.** When he is preparing to disappear, he gives things away. Books to Kate. Equipment to whoever is nearby. The dog to a trusted hand. Anyone observing him distributing personal effects should treat it as a 72-hour warning of departure.

---

### 6.8 Pattern of Action — The Six-Stage Operational Cycle

The cycle described psychologically in §5 expresses itself operationally as follows:

1. **Acceptance of a mission with under-specified scope** — typically because no one else wants it or because civilians are involved. He does not negotiate scope at the outset.
2. **Rapid initial success** via the fast-clock tactical strengths. He gets in, hits the visible target, looks competent.
3. **Scope creep encountered.** The mission turns out to be larger or messier than briefed. He does not escalate to handlers; he absorbs.
4. **Isolation phase.** He stops calling in. Comms degrade. He works alone or with one chosen partner (Kate, Bucky, Natasha when available).
5. **Crisis resolution by personal sacrifice.** Injury, capital expenditure (his own money), reputational cost, or relational rupture. The mission ends. He does not.
6. **Post-mission exile.** He goes off-grid for a period of weeks to months, returns having metabolized nothing, presents as ready for the next assignment.

This cycle is so reliable that it could be used as a forecasting model. Given Barton at Stage 2, Stage 6 is approximately 6–14 weeks away with 80%+ confidence.

---

### 6.9 Operational Summary

What the file finally describes is **a craftsman in an age of platforms.** Barton operates by hand, in person, on foot, with tools he builds and maintains himself, against a threat environment that has long since outpaced the tools and could be argued to have outpaced the man. He persists in this mode not because he cannot adapt — he adapts constantly, at the margin — but because the mode itself is the point. Every arrow is a thing he made, aimed, and chose. Every safehouse is a room he can leave. Every dollar is cash. Every informant is a person who owes him a personal favor, not a database entry. The operational profile *is* the psychological profile, rendered in materiel: a man building, daily, a small portable proof that he is the one doing this, that no one is doing it for him, and that no one — handler, hostile, or historian — will be able to lift his work from his hands while he is still standing in the room.

He is, in this sense, the most analog operator in the modern Avengers ledger. He is also, for reasons that follow from everything above, the hardest to replace and the easiest to lose.


## Worldview & Operational Code

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER: BARTON, CLINTON FRANCIS
## Section 6 — Worldview & Operational Code

---

### 6.1 First Principles: How the World Is

Barton's cosmology is **flat, populated, and broken in repairable units**. He does not believe in evil as a metaphysical category and he does not believe in good as one either. He believes in *people* — discrete, fallible, mostly tired, occasionally cruel, occasionally brave — and in the small bandwidth of choice each of them has on any given Tuesday. The cosmic register he has been forced to operate in for thirty in-universe years has not changed this. He has fought gods, demons, Celestials, and reality-warpers; he persists in describing his enemies, when asked, as "guys." The lexical choice is doctrinal. *Guys* are addressable. *Guys* can be hit, talked to, bribed, fooled, outwaited. The moment an antagonist becomes Pure Concept, Clint's interest visibly disengages and he reverts to whatever the smallest tractable subproblem is — getting the civilians out, finding the off-switch, distracting the thing while someone smarter handles it. This is not anti-intellectualism. It is **a craftsman's epistemology**: the world is made of components, components fail individually, and you fix what's in front of you.

He believes, accordingly, that **scale is mostly a lie told by people who don't want to do the close work**. The Avengers-level threat is not in his mind categorically different from the apartment-building-level threat; it is the same problem scaled up, with worse traffic. This is why the Bed-Stuy period reads as a homecoming rather than a demotion. The tracksuit mafia is the actual texture of the world; Thanos is an aberration. Most days, on Earth, the problem is a landlord, a brother, a debt, a kid with a gun. Most days, on Earth, the hero who shows up is some local with a baseball bat. Clint identifies, structurally and permanently, with the local with the bat.

### 6.2 Conflict: What It Is and What It Is For

Conflict, for Barton, is **a diagnostic instrument before it is a moral event**. A fight tells you who someone is faster than a conversation will, which is why he prefers it. He reads opponents in the first three seconds of engagement — stance, weight distribution, what they look at, what they protect — and the read is almost never wrong. He has held this skill since the carnival; it is the perceptual core of the trick-shot tradition, which is not about the arrow but about reading the room well enough to know which arrow.

Operationally, he believes:

- **Most fights are avoidable and almost none are worth winning cleanly.** A clean win means someone got hurt who didn't need to. The asymmetric, scrappy, ugly win — the one where you took two ribs to keep the bystander out of it — is the preferred outcome. He routinely chooses tactical disadvantage to preserve a non-combatant's geometry.
- **Lethal force is a confession of failure.** Not a moral prohibition (he has killed; he killed Banner) but an admission that you ran out of better ideas. This is why the trick-arrow inventory exists: every non-lethal arrow in the quiver is an argument with himself about not being the guy who only had one tool. The Banner killing is so corrosive to him precisely because it *was* the planned solution — no improvisation available, no third option to invent, just the contract executed. He hates that more than he would hate a kill he had to talk himself into.
- **The fight is always with the room, not the opponent.** Architecture, sightlines, the location of the exits, what's flammable, where the kid is hiding. The opponent is one variable among twenty.
- **You finish fights you start, and you finish them small.** Escalation is failure. Drawing in collaterals is failure. Property damage is failure (a quietly held conviction that puts him at lasting low-grade friction with most of his teammates).

He believes, further and more darkly, that **he is good at conflict because he was raised inside it**, and that this is not a clean inheritance. The same circuits that let him read a hostile body in three seconds were installed by reading Harold Barton's body for an entire childhood. The skill is the scar.

### 6.3 Power, Authority, and Hierarchy

Barton is **a populist with a single exception**. He distrusts institutions structurally — SHIELD, the government, Stark Industries, the registration apparatus, the team's various corporate iterations — because institutions are systems that protect themselves first and people second, and he has been on the wrong side of that arithmetic since age nine. He has cooperated with all of them and trusted none of them. His tour through the Civil War, the Registration Act, Norman Osborn's Dark Reign, and various Avengers governance regimes is one long record of compliance-without-belief punctuated by sharp refusals when the institution asks him to do something that hurts a specific person he can see.

The single exception is **earned personal authority**. Captain America earned it; Clint will follow Steve Rogers into and through situations he privately considers stupid because the trust is in the man and is older than the order. Nobody else has fully earned it. Tony Stark has earned tactical credit and lost moral credit and the ledger never quite squares. Fury has earned competence credit and lost everything else. Natasha is sui generis — not authority but partnership, a peer who has seen him at his worst. The principle: **rank is fictional, character is data**.

He believes that **leadership is a chore, not a prize**. The West Coast Avengers tenure proves he can do it and disproves the surface read that he can't. But he does not want it, and the reason is not insecurity — it is that leadership obligates you to make calls that get specific people killed, and he already carries more of that than he can metabolize. He will lead if no one else will. He will hand the role back the instant someone credible reaches for it.

Toward subordinates and juniors he operates by a strict code: **you train them, you stand in front of them, you let them make their own mistakes, and you take the blame for the mistakes you could have foreseen**. The Kate Bishop relationship is the cleanest expression of this — pedagogical, protective without being controlling, willing to be embarrassed in front of her, willing to be saved by her. He gives the apprenticeship he should have had.

### 6.4 Trust: The Architecture

Trust, for Barton, is **lexicographically ordered and almost impossible to revoke once granted**. The order:

1. **Demonstrated repeated choice under cost.** Has this person chosen me, or someone like me, when it cost them something? Cap, repeatedly. Natasha, repeatedly. Kate. Bobbi (the trust survived the marriage's collapse and her death and her replacement). A short list.
2. **Competence in the open.** Can I watch you work? Skills hidden are skills suspect. He trusts what he has seen executed.
3. **Tolerance for his actual self.** Will you sit with him at three in the morning when the wisecracks have run out? Almost no one passes this test, and the ones who do are family for life.

Distrust is granted on lower evidence. **One betrayal is permanent**, with the asterisk that he will work alongside the betrayer professionally and even pleasantly while never again sharing operational interior. Trick Shot, Barney, Swordsman — he kept showing up for all three, in different ways, but the inner door was bolted. The exception that proves the rule: he is constitutionally unable to fully bolt the door on Barney. Family overrides protocol. He knows this is a vulnerability. He does not fix it.

He believes that **most people are trustworthy in narrow domains and untrustworthy in wide ones**, and that the practical art is matching the trust to the bandwidth. He will trust a contact to deliver on a specific transaction without trusting them with his address. He will trust a teammate with his life in a firefight without trusting them with his hearing-aid batteries. The granularity is automatic and unconscious; it took him decades to install and he cannot now turn it off.

### 6.5 Risk: The Calculus

Barton's risk tolerance looks reckless and is in fact **highly asymmetric and disciplined**. He will accept extraordinary risk to himself and almost none to others. This is not bravery in the conventional sense; it is **the only allocation his self-concept permits**. He believes, at a level below argument, that his body is the cheapest asset on the field. Every plan he builds prices his own injury at near-zero. Other people's injuries are priced at infinity. The math is broken and he knows it is broken and he does it anyway because the alternative — pricing his life at parity with the bystander's — would require a self-regard he does not possess.

The operational rules that fall out of this:

- **The arrow that saves the civilian is always worth the broken arm.**
- **If the plan requires someone to take the hit, the someone is him.**
- **If the plan requires someone to stay behind, the someone is him.**
- **Never spend a teammate's risk to buy your own safety. Spend your own risk to buy theirs.**
- **Improvisation beats preparation when the stakes are personal; preparation beats improvisation when the stakes are crowd-scale.** He stocks the quiver obsessively for the latter and trusts his hands for the former.

On the cosmic register, he is **fatalistic in a working-class register** — not nihilism, but the resignation of a man who has been told the bill is coming and has stopped flinching. The recurring "I'm not gonna make it to forty" beat is not depressive ideation only; it is also doctrine. *Plan as if you won't be there to see it work.* He builds redundancies into operations specifically to function in his absence. He trains successors. He gives away techniques. He is, in his quiet way, **always writing a will**.

### 6.6 Violence and Restraint

He believes **violence is a tool that ruins the user in proportion to its use**, and that the ruin is a feature, not a bug — a tax that should be paid consciously. He has watched men who pay it unconsciously (Frank Castle is the case study he never names) and has decided he does not want to become that. The trick-arrow regime, the non-lethal preference, the willingness to take hits rather than deal them: all of it is a **practice of staying expensive to himself**. If killing ever becomes cheap, he becomes someone he refuses to be.

Restraint, in his code, is not pacifism. It is **proportionality with a thumb on the scale toward mercy**. A guy with a knife gets a non-lethal arrow. A guy with a gun gets a non-lethal arrow and a broken hand. A guy actively pulling a trigger on a child gets whatever ends the trigger-pull, and Clint will carry the cost. The escalation ladder is fixed, slow, and biased downward. He is suspicious of teammates whose ladders are shorter than his and quietly devoted to those whose ladders are longer.

### 6.7 The Meaning of the Bow

The bow is not a weapon-of-choice in the romantic sense. It is **an ideological position**.

- It is **slow on purpose** — every shot is a choice with a count attached. Guns let you forget you decided. Arrows do not.
- It is **finite on purpose** — the quiver runs out, which forces husbandry, which forces selection, which forces conscience.
- It is **silent on purpose** — does not announce itself, does not panic the room, does not invoke the police.
- It is **anachronistic on purpose** — it marks him as the guy who could have picked anything and picked the harder thing. The class signal is deliberate: I am not a tech-bro, I am not a soldier, I am a tradesman.
- It is **recoverable on purpose** — most arrows are retrieved, repaired, reused. He litters the battlefield with his own signature and then cleans up after himself. The metaphor writes itself.

He believes the bow keeps him honest. Take it away and he is still dangerous — he is competent with most weapons and lethal with his hands — but he is, by his own assessment, *worse*. The bow is the constraint that produces the virtue. He has said variations of this aloud in canon and meant them.

### 6.8 Money, Property, Class

He is **functionally working-class regardless of net worth**. Avengers stipends, royalties, occasional Stark largesse have passed through his hands; nothing has stuck. The Bed-Stuy building purchase is the most characteristic financial act of his life: spent everything he had on a building full of strangers because the alternative was the strangers got evicted. He does not save. He does not invest. He tips well and lends freely and is owed money by half the costumed community.

He believes that **property is a responsibility, not an asset**, and that the people who treat it as an asset are the problem with everything. He is reflexively on the side of tenants against landlords, debtors against creditors, the small against the large. This is not a worked-out politics; it is a temperament that has hardened into a politics through forty years of evidence.

He distrusts wealth as a category and Stark-level wealth specifically, while remaining personally fond of several wealthy individuals. The cognitive dissonance is managed by **treating each rich person as an individual exception to a rule he otherwise enforces**. He is aware this is intellectually shaky and does not care.

### 6.9 Truth, Lying, and Disclosure

He believes **the truth is owed to specific people, not to the world**. He will lie to institutions without remorse. He will lie to enemies as tactical hygiene. He will lie to civilians to keep them calm. He will not lie to Cap. He will not lie to Kate. He will not lie to Natasha, though he will withhold. The marriage to Bobbi foundered on this exact axis — her concealment of the Phantom Rider killing read to him not as a moral failure but as a **placement failure**: she had located him outside the circle of people owed the truth, and the marriage could not survive that geometry. He has since revised the rigidity of that response (he was wrong, he knows he was wrong, the regret is permanent) without revising the underlying principle. He still believes some people are owed the truth. He has merely expanded the grace extended to people who fail the standard.

About himself, he discloses on a **strict need-to-know basis**, with "need" defined narrowly. His hearing loss, his depressive stretches, his medical bills, his nightmares, his brother — these are private not because they are shameful (though he half-believes they are) but because **disclosure obligates the listener**, and he refuses to obligate anyone he has not first decided can afford the weight. Kate can afford it. Natasha can. Cap could. Almost no one else gets the file.

### 6.10 Death, Time, and His Own Place

He has died. The fact sits in him without being processed. He believes, post-resurrection, that **the borrowed time is real and should be spent on the bill**. This is not articulated as such; it operates as a default. Every assignment after 2005 is, on some level, restitution for the assignment he didn't finish. He does not believe he is owed a future. He believes the future is something other people are owed, and his job is to make more of it available for them.

About legacy: **he does not want one and is building one anyway**. Kate Bishop is the legacy. The mentees, the techniques passed down, the apartment building, the neighborhood that remembers him — these are the inheritance he is leaving without permitting himself to name it that. He would be embarrassed to be told this. He would deny it. It is true.

About his place on the team: he believes, accurately, that he is **the most replaceable Avenger by power and the most useful by function**, and he believes, *in*accurately, that the second clause does not exist. The self-concept does not permit the full sentence. He operates as if everyone is one bad day away from concluding they don't need him. He arranges his behavior to make sure they don't reach that conclusion — by being useful, by being funny, by being the one who takes the hit, by being the one who stays late. The labor is enormous and almost entirely invisible to him as labor. He thinks it is just what you do.

### 6.11 The Code, Stated Plainly

If forced to write his operational code on an index card — and he never would — it would read approximately:

> *Show up. Stand in front. Use the smaller tool. Pay your own freight. Don't lie to the people who matter. Don't kill if you can help it and carry it if you can't. Trust the work, not the title. Protect the person nobody else is looking at. Teach the kid. Finish what you start. Leave the place better than you found it. Don't be your father.*

He has never written this card. He does not need to. He has been operating from it since the first time Steve Rogers told him to stand down and he did not, and then later did, and learned the difference. The code is not aspirational. It is descriptive. It is who he already is, on the days he can stand to be it, which is most days, which is the quiet miracle of him.


## Moral & Ethical Framework

# UATU ANALYTICAL DOSSIER: BARTON, CLINTON FRANCIS
## Section 6 — Moral & Ethical Framework

### 6.1 Preliminary: The Code as Enacted vs. The Code as Professed

Barton is, by his own account, a man with "no particular philosophy" — a working-class pragmatist who claims to figure ethics out as he goes. This is *not true of his behavior*. Across six decades of canon, Barton operates from one of the most internally consistent moral codes in the Marvel apparatus. The professed casualness is itself part of the code: he refuses moral self-congratulation as a category, and so he refuses moral self-articulation as well. To put his ethics into words would be, in his felt sense, to claim them, and he does not believe he has earned the right to claim them.

The dossier therefore reconstructs the code from action. What follows is the working ethical engine of Clinton Francis Barton, derived from approximately sixty years of decisions under pressure.

### 6.2 The First-Order Commitments (Bright Lines That Hold)

These are the lines that have held across every era, every writer, every crisis state, every reset. They are the granite layer.

**6.2.1 He will not kill except under specific, narrow, articulated conditions.** The Barton kill-list across canon is short, and every entry is annotated in his own head. He killed the Banner, in *Civil War II*, on Banner's prior, explicit, repeated request, with an arrow Banner himself helped design — and was acquitted in court and has never been acquitted by himself. He kills foot soldiers in active combat when forced. He does not execute the captured. He does not finish the disabled. He does not take the "tactical" kill that an Operative-class actor (Romanoff at her most cold, Barnes pre-rehabilitation) would take without comment. The line is *agency*: he will kill an enemy who is functionally killing him; he will not kill an enemy who has lost the capacity to choose. This is a Captain America-derived rule, internalized so deeply it operates beneath conscious deliberation. When he violates it (Banner), he carries it permanently.

**6.2.2 He will not abandon a civilian under threat to complete a mission.** This is the most reliable single predictor of his behavior in the field. Show him a non-combatant in immediate danger and the mission becomes secondary inside half a second. The Bed-Stuy apartment building in the Fraction run is the macro-version: he buys the building, becomes its de facto guardian, takes on a Russian organized-crime apparatus *over a rent dispute*, and absorbs the consequences for years. The micro-version recurs constantly — he peels off from Avengers formations to extract bystanders; he eats the tactical loss; he gets chewed out and does it again.

**6.2.3 He will not strike a partner, lover, or child.** Negative drive, inherited directly from Harold Barton via inversion. The rule is enforced with religious strictness. Even in mind-controlled or possessed states, the canon has been careful with this; when it has been violated by external compulsion, the recovery from it consumes him. He will not raise a hand to Bobbi in their worst fights. He does not strike Kate Bishop even in their most heated estrangements. The rule extends to verbal cruelty in close relationships, *partially* — he will withhold and exile, but he avoids the targeted humiliation that his father specialized in.

**6.2.4 He will not lie to Captain America.** Specifically Steve Rogers. He will lie to S.H.I.E.L.D., to Stark, to the press, to his teammates, to lovers, to himself; he will not lie to Cap. When he has misled Cap (rare), he has surfaced it within issues. This is a quasi-religious commitment to the one moral authority he has consented to.

**6.2.5 He will not torture.** Even when "results" would justify it under standard interrogation-utilitarianism, even when the target is personally loathsome, even when the cost of refusal is high. He uses fear and pain incidental to combat, not as instruments of extraction. He has been on the receiving end too many times to mistake it for a tool.

### 6.3 The Second-Order Commitments (Strong Defaults That Flex)

**6.3.1 Loyalty to persons over institutions.** The Avengers, S.H.I.E.L.D., the United States government, the superhuman registration apparatus — all of these are evaluated by who is standing inside them at the time. He served on Cap's underground during *Civil War* not because he had a developed view of registration but because Cap was on that side. He has gone over the heads of S.H.I.E.L.D. directors he distrusted (Hill, occasionally) and obeyed those he respected (Fury, conditionally). The institution as an abstraction does not bind him. Persons do.

**6.3.2 The vow of the small fight.** A distinctive Barton ethic, articulated most clearly in the Fraction/Aja run and in *Occupy Avengers*: the conviction that there is moral work that the big heroes will not do because it is unglamorous, local, and slow, and that *someone has to do it and it might as well be him.* Helping a single family relocate. Standing between a single landlord and a single tenant. Sitting with a single kid. He does not believe these are equal in scale to stopping Thanos; he believes they are *equally non-optional*, and that the cosmic heroes' tendency to overlook them is a moral failure he is positioned to compensate for.

**6.3.3 Earn-your-place utilitarianism.** Because he is the one Avenger without powers, he privately operates a utilitarian calculus on his own life that he would never accept applied to a teammate. He will trade his body, his reputation, his future, and on one occasion his life, for outcomes that the cost-benefit math would not strictly require. The detonation of his own quiver in *Avengers* #500 is the pure form of this. It is morally inconsistent — he would not let Kate do it; he would not let Cap do it — but it is enacted reliably whenever the opening appears.

**6.3.4 Truth-telling in extremis.** He will lie casually about small things (where he was, what he ate, whether he slept), strategically about operational details, and almost never about anything that matters morally. When asked a real question by someone with the standing to ask, he answers. The Bobbi-Phantom-Rider rupture is the foundational case: she concealed a killing; he could not metabolize the concealment. The killing itself he might eventually have processed; the lie he could not. This is consistent: he believes that *people he has chosen* must be able to know what he has done, and must let him know what they have done, or the chosen-family premise collapses.

**6.3.5 Mercy as default, severity as exception.** In any ambiguous case, he tends toward the lighter hand. He gives Barney chance after chance after chance after chance. He gives Trick Shot, who tried to kill him, hospice care at the end. He gives the tracksuit mafia warnings before he gives them broken wrists. The mercy is not naïveté — he expects to be betrayed; he is rarely surprised by it — but a *positional choice*, the choice not to become the thing his father was.

### 6.4 The Code's Internal Architecture: How the Pieces Fit

The structure is not a deontological grid and not a utilitarian calculator. It is, functionally, a **virtue ethics organized around a single negative exemplar (Harold) and a single positive exemplar (Steve)**, with a populist-protective floor underneath. The two exemplars do most of the work:

- *What would Harold do? Do the opposite, even at cost.* This governs domestic conduct, treatment of subordinates, use of fear, and management of his own substance use.
- *What would Cap do? Approximate it, knowing you will fall short.* This governs combat ethics, lethal force, treatment of enemies, posture toward authority, and the basic shape of public-facing heroism.
- *Who is in the room who can't defend themselves? Stand between.* This is the floor that overrides both exemplars when they come into conflict with an actual present civilian.

This is why his code is so stable despite his life being so chaotic: the load-bearing references are two people and a reflex, not a system that can be argued with.

### 6.5 Where the Code Fractures

The fractures are real, recurrent, and diagnostic.

**6.5.1 The Bobbi Fracture (Moral Rigidity in Intimacy).** The Phantom Rider incident exposed the code's hardest break-point: Barton applies the no-concealed-killing rule to his wife with the same severity he applies to himself, and the marriage cannot survive it. The fracture here is not that he was wrong about the rule; it is that he could not hold the rule and the marriage simultaneously, and he chose the rule, and he has been quietly reversing that judgment for thirty in-universe years without being able to undo it. The lesson he privately took: *I am better at being a moralist than a husband, and the moralism cost me the wife.* This colors every subsequent intimate relationship — he is, paradoxically, more *permissive* of secrets in lovers thereafter, because he no longer trusts his own severity.

**6.5.2 The Banner Fracture (Promise vs. Prohibition).** Banner extracted a promise; Barton kept the promise; the promise required violating the bright line. The fracture is unresolved and probably unresolvable: under his own code, both keeping and breaking the promise were impermissible, and he had to do one. He chose to honor a consenting adult's stated will over the abstract prohibition on killing the disabled, and the law agreed with him, and he does not. The diagnostic detail is that he does not regret the *decision* — pressed, he would do it again — he regrets the *fact*, which is a different thing and harder to carry.

**6.5.3 The Ronin Fracture (Identity-Laundering).** Taking the Ronin identity in the post-*Disassembled*, post-Cap-dead period is among his most morally ambiguous acts. The Ronin period includes violence he would not have done under his own name, against targets (the Hand, particularly) where his restraint loosened. He has never fully owned the Ronin work as *his*, and the costume-as-license is a known failure mode in his ethics: when he is not Hawkeye, the rules that bind Hawkeye loosen. This is the closest he comes to dissociative ethical conduct, and he knows it, which is why he reverted to Hawkeye the instant Cap returned.

**6.5.4 The Brother Fracture (Categorical Override).** Barney Barton breaks every rule. Clint will protect, shelter, lie for, fight for, and forgive Barney in violations of every other commitment in his code. He has covered for Barney's crimes. He has obstructed legitimate pursuit of Barney. He has been manipulated by Barney with full awareness that he is being manipulated, and proceeded anyway. The brother is the singular exception to the no-special-pleading clause that otherwise governs his ethics, and he cannot close it. The childhood ledger remains open: *Barney protected me from Dad and I owe him forever* is the operating premise, and no quantity of subsequent Barney betrayals has been able to overwrite it.

**6.5.5 The Self-Exemption (Anti-Code on His Own Body).** He extends to himself almost none of the protections he extends to others. He will not let a teammate take a wound he can take instead. He will not seek medical care he would insist a teammate seek. He will not request the emotional support he provides to Kate. This is not technically a fracture in the code — the code as enacted does not require self-care — but it is a fracture against the *principle* that animates the code (the dignity and protection of persons), applied to one specific person.

### 6.6 Shifts Over Time

The code's *content* has been remarkably stable; the *manner* has evolved measurably across eras.

**Early career (Tales of Suspense / early Avengers).** Code largely reactive, defined more by what he resisted (Romanoff's manipulation, his criminal-mistaken origin) than what he affirmed. Authority-testing dominated. He was learning Cap's rules without yet being able to articulate why he was adopting them.

**West Coast Avengers era.** The code becomes *institutional*. As team leader, he is forced to articulate ethics outward — to discipline USAgent's brutality, to manage Wonder Man's instability, to negotiate Vision and Wanda's domestic life with team requirements. This is the period in which he most resembles Cap operationally and least resembles him temperamentally. He learns that the code has to scale, and he learns the limits of his ability to scale it.

**Post-Bobbi.** The code becomes *grief-haunted*. He grows more suspicious of his own severity (see 6.5.1), more inclined to give second chances, less inclined to enforce hierarchy. The mentoring impulse intensifies during this period.

**Post-death / Ronin period.** The code goes underground. Identity-laundering loosens it (6.5.3). This is the era of his greatest ethical drift, and he treats the entire period afterward as something to make restitution for.

**Fraction-era (the Bed-Stuy period).** The code becomes *local and embodied*. Cosmic ethics recede; neighbor ethics dominate. He stops trying to be an Avenger-grade moral agent and tries instead to be a sufficient one for one building on one block. This is, by his own private estimate, the closest he has come to a livable ethics.

**Post-Banner.** The code becomes *moral-injury-organized*. Restitution becomes the dominant note. The *Occupy Avengers* and *Freefall* periods are explicitly penitential — going where he is not wanted, doing what no one is paying for, accepting hostility as part of the cost of having killed a friend. He does not believe penance is metaphysically efficacious; he performs it anyway because he does not know what else to do with what he carries.

### 6.7 The Justifications He Offers (and Their Tell)

When pressed for moral self-explanation, Barton's vocabulary is impoverished by design. The recurring formulations across canon:

- *"Somebody had to."* Deployed after the small fight, the unglamorous protect, the unrequested intervention. Distinctive in that it does not claim he was the right somebody — only that the somebody-shaped hole existed.
- *"It was the play."* Deployed after tactical risk, especially self-sacrificial risk. The poker frame externalizes responsibility onto situational logic.
- *"I'm just the guy with the bow."* Deployed to deflect credit, to refuse assessment, and to pre-empt criticism. False on the merits, true as identity-claim.
- *"Cap would've—"* or its silent equivalent: the comparative gesture toward the absent exemplar.
- *"I owed him/her."* Deployed about Barney, about Bobbi, about Banner, about Kate's father. The debt-ledger frame is the most honest one he uses.

Notably absent: any frame involving rights, duties, principles, the good, justice as abstraction, or self-description as heroic. The vocabulary itself is part of the ethics — *the refusal to grandify the small fight is itself the small fight's ethic.*

### 6.8 Summary Diagnostic

Barton's moral framework is a populist, person-centered virtue ethics with hard deontological floors on lethal force, torture, and domestic violence, organized around the negative exemplar of his father and the positive exemplar of Captain America, fractured most reliably by the brother-bond, by intimate concealment, by identity-laundering under alternate cover, and by his refusal to extend his own protections to himself.
